I've never seen a Linux distribution that didn't include static versions
of the timezones. Setting the timezone to PST instead of US/Pacific will
make the system clock be PST all the time. I don't know whether the other
bit of software can handle that, but I think it likely.

> Also, I like the extra daylight at the end of the day during DST..

Then arrange with your employer to start and end your day early during
the summer. Don't mess with my clock (not to mention the huge
productivity losses due to sleepy workers during the change).

On Mon, 12 Mar 2007, Andrew Grover wrote:

> Check out this thread: http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=91948

   All this does is present the Ubuntu version of su-ing to root and runing
chmod on devices. Nothing to see there.

> Once you can identify your device,

   And this is what I'm trying to do.

   In the 2.4.x kernel days (and earlier), I could test connections (e.g.,
for my PDA) by trying ttyS0-ttyS3. Same with the USB thumb: I could try
mounting ttyUSB0-ttyUSB3 or sda/sda1/sdb/sdb1, etc. until I discovered the
device. With the 2.6.x kernel I've no idea what devices to try. There are no
/dev/sd* or /dev/ttyUSB*.

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Mar 13 07:57:28 2007
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)

Subject: [PLUG] Gentoo
In-Reply-To: <20070313014628.bac2f52a.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Tue, 13 Mar 2007, John Jason Jordan wrote:

> Date: Tue, 13 Mar 2007 01:46:28 -0700
> From: John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net>
> Reply-To: "General Linux/UNIX discussion and help;	civil and on-topic"
>     <plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> To: PLUG <plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> Subject: [PLUG] Gentoo
> 
> I want to try Gentoo amd64 version with a live/install CD. For the life
> of me I cannot figure out which file to download:
>
> http://torrents.gentoo.org/
>
> None of the files seem to be .iso files. Nor can I find any
> documentation that says the file is a live CD, an install CD, or both.
>
> Any suggestions?

In all my gentoo builds (except perhaps the first one or two), I have
used a knoppix CD to boot from.  A knoppix CD gives you a better
runtime environment than a gentoo LiveCD.  During the build process,
you use wget, lynx or links to download 2 items: the portage tree and
the stage3 tarball.  The Gentoo Handbook gives you the URL to the
gentoo mirror page at those points in the narrative where you are
meant to download these items.

http://mirror.wrlug.org/knoppix/ is a site-of-last-resort for getting
knoppix.  It's my web server, running on Cox Cable, therefore it's not
terribly fast.

I also have an amd64.  It makes no difference that the knoppix CD is a
32-bit build.  You do all your compiling in a chroot, which is 64-bit.

Carlos Konstanski

John,

On Tue, Mar 13, 2007 at 12:31:03PM -0700, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> Now I'm doing OpenSuse 10.2 amd64. However, that will take all day
> because it comes only on DVD or multiple CDs.

This is incorrect, you can do installs over the network using the mini
iso:

  ftp://ftp.osuosl.org/.1/opensuse/opensuse/distribution/10.2/iso/cd/openSUSE-10.2-GM-x86_64-mini.iso

See http://en.opensuse.org/INSTALL_Internet .

(You can also do a network install using just a vmlinuz image and
initrd, see http://en.opensuse.org/Installation_without_CD )

I hope you find a distribution that meets your needs.

This is a Thunderbird error message I get when I try to get messages
from my work e-mail service on my laptop. The situation is that they use
pop.secureserver.net for incoming e-mail to haskinsnw.com. As the new
kid on the block (I changed real estate companies), and the only one who
uses Thunderbird, I'm the first one to have this problem. Everyone else
uses "lookout" which apparently is not concerned with security problems
like this. My question is, can I somehow tell Thunderbird that this
mismatch is OK? Once I click on "OK" T-bird gets my messages. If I can't
tell T-bird to ignore this, I won't be able to have automatic checking
and retrieving enabled.

Here's the complete message:

-----------------------------------------------------------------------
Security Error: Domain Name Mismatch

You have attempted to establish a connection with "mail.haskinsnw.com".
However, the security certificate presented belongs to
"pop.secureserver.net". It is possible, though unlikely, that someone
may be trying to intercept your communication with this web site.

If you suspect the certificate shown does not belong to
"mail.haskinsnw.com', please cancel the connection and notify the site
administrator."
-----------------------------------------------------------------------

TIA

Change your POP hostname to use pop.secureserver.net, then the cert will
be valid. Yes, thunderbird will always complain, as it should :)

mail.haskinsnw.com is an alias for pop.secureserver.net.
pop.secureserver.net has address 64.202.165.92

Never one to let a surreal challenge pass, Keith responds:

Chauvinist!  Time should only be expressed as a 256 bit binary number,
the number of Planck time units ( approximately 5.39121E-44 sec) since
the formation of the One True Quark soon after the Big Bang, and
following the relativistic worldline of that quark.

The One True Quark is located in a hydrogen atom in the cartilage of
my left big toe.  The correct time is:

Bing! 0x00000000000004FA793930BCD112280EBB121164A32C86C88E8D4A516F7DB1A7

Note, there will be a rollover problem at 2^256 Planck times, about
197.8 trillion trillion years from now.  Please make sure your 256
bit Planck-time system libraries properly handle this special case.
Don't forget to account for Proton Savings Time, when you will be
required by law to adjust your clock forwards by one Planck time
at every even rollover, and backwards every odd rollover.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Mar 15 11:05:45 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Thu, 15 Mar 2007 11:05:45 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Identifying USB Device Used by Hardware -- RESOLVED
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0703121804070.20379@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0703121804070.20379@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0703151101140.14318@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Mon, 12 Mar 2007, Rich Shepard wrote:

>  My scanner is now plugged into a USB port on the rear of the motherboard,
> along with a web cam. Because of permissions problems, I -- a mere user --
> cannot use them. I will change permissions to allow me access to these
> devices, but to do so I need to know to what device they're assigned.

   I resolved this issue. The Xsane FAQ had the answer: the permissions on
/proc/bus/usb/001/00x were restricted to root. Changing that to a+rw solved
the problem.

   On a more permanent basis (since that goes away when the scanner is
unplugged/turned off or the system rebooted), I added users to the scanner group
in /etc/group.

   Once that was done, Xsane found the device.

   BTW, if anyone is using xsane-0.993 and does not have color management
built in, the application crashes as soon as it starts to scan. Go to the
xsane.org web site and grab the source tarball for 0.994 where that problem
(and a PostScript issue) has been fixed. Slackware-11.0 package available.

Rich

From johnxj at comcast.net  Thu Mar 15 15:33:22 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Thu, 15 Mar 2007 15:33:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Torrents of confusion
Message-ID: <20070315153322.8780446f.johnxj@comcast.net>

I have been downloading live/install ISOs for differrent distros lately. I use Ktorrent, which allows me to see a lot of detail about what is going on. My cable connection here is generally good -- if a server can send me data fast enough I have seen download speeds of 5-600K/s and upload speeds of 150K/s or so. I understand the basics of how torrents work, but the download/upload results I get with torrents are not making any sense in a number of ways.

As I write this I am doing the following:
F7-test2-livecd-i386.iso 27 seeders, 2 leechers, avg download speed 25K/s, avg upload speed .5K/s, share ratio .02
*feisty-desktop-amd64.iso, 2 peers, avg download speed 56K/s, avg upload speed 8.5K/s, share ratio 7.83
*livecd-amd64-installer-2006.1 [Gentoo], 1 peer, avg download speed 43K/s, avg upload speed 5K/s 1.23
*openSUSE-10.2-GM-DVD-x86_64.iso, 48 peers, avg download speed 81K/s, avg upload speed 30.5K/s, share ratio .94
*ubuntu-6.06.1-desktop-i386, 2 peers, avg upload speed 3.5K/s, share ratio 1.01

* = 100% downloaded, just seeding

There are several things that do not make sense:

1) I started all these downloads about five days ago. Why have I shared 7.83 times for feisty and only 1.01 for the i386 of Dapper? Surely the i386 of Dapper would be way more popular than the amd64 of feisty, right?

2) Why is F7-test2 downloading at only 25K/s when there are 27 seeders with 100% to offer, and only two leechers besides me?

3) At this moment I am averaging a total of 30-35K/s upload and 25K/s download, yet my internet connection is lagged into next week. When I click to send this e-mail it will take about 1.5 minutes to find the server, another minute for authentication and handshaking, and another minute to transmit. That's for ONE e-mail, a process that normally takes 15-20 seconds. And web pages load so slowly the glaciers will be completely melted before some of them appear. I have enough bandwidth that the up-and downloading I am doing should be almost unnoticeable. And I know it's something with the torrents because if I close down Ktorrent my internet connection goes back to normal. I don't even have to close it down -- just hitting the Stop button returns me to normal operation. What is sucking up my bandwidth?


From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Thu Mar 15 15:41:29 2007
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason Martin)
Date: Thu, 15 Mar 2007 15:41:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Torrents of confusion
In-Reply-To: <20070315153322.8780446f.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070315153322.8780446f.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <c29537840703151541x6f67899du91eabe363c247c93@mail.gmail.com>

On 3/15/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> I have been downloading live/install ISOs for differrent distros lately. I use Ktorrent, which allows me to see a lot of detail about what is going on. My cable connection here is generally good -- if a server can send me data fast enough I have seen download speeds of 5-600K/s and upload speeds of 150K/s or so. I understand the basics of how torrents work, but the download/upload results I get with torrents are not making any sense in a number of ways.
>
> As I write this I am doing the following:
> F7-test2-livecd-i386.iso 27 seeders, 2 leechers, avg download speed 25K/s, avg upload speed .5K/s, share ratio .02
> *feisty-desktop-amd64.iso, 2 peers, avg download speed 56K/s, avg upload speed 8.5K/s, share ratio 7.83
> *livecd-amd64-installer-2006.1 [Gentoo], 1 peer, avg download speed 43K/s, avg upload speed 5K/s 1.23
> *openSUSE-10.2-GM-DVD-x86_64.iso, 48 peers, avg download speed 81K/s, avg upload speed 30.5K/s, share ratio .94
> *ubuntu-6.06.1-desktop-i386, 2 peers, avg upload speed 3.5K/s, share ratio 1.01
>
> * = 100% downloaded, just seeding
>
> There are several things that do not make sense:
>
> 1) I started all these downloads about five days ago. Why have I shared 7.83 times for feisty and only 1.01 for the i386 of Dapper? Surely the i386 of Dapper would be way more popular than the amd64 of feisty, right?
>
> 2) Why is F7-test2 downloading at only 25K/s when there are 27 seeders with 100% to offer, and only two leechers besides me?
>
> 3) At this moment I am averaging a total of 30-35K/s upload and 25K/s download, yet my internet connection is lagged into next week. When I click to send this e-mail it will take about 1.5 minutes to find the server, another minute for authentication and handshaking, and another minute to transmit. That's for ONE e-mail, a process that normally takes 15-20 seconds. And web pages load so slowly the glaciers will be completely melted before some of them appear. I have enough bandwidth that the up-and downloading I am doing should be almost unnoticeable. And I know it's something with the torrents because if I close down Ktorrent my internet connection goes back to normal. I don't even have to close it down -- just hitting the Stop button returns me to normal operation. What is sucking up my bandwidth?

Probably the uploads, believe it or not.  TCP requires acknowledgments
to operate correctly and includes a backoff mechanism when it detects
congestion based on drops and retransmissions.  If your uploads are
sucking up all your upstream bandwidth (which is less than downstream
bandwidth for most broadband connections), then ACK messages will get
lost/dropped, and congestion control will kick in.

Bittorrent is a great concept, but unless you have a router that does
quality of service traffic shaping, bittorrent uploads will definitely
kill your outbound traffic speeds, and by doing so will also kill your
download speeds.

Jason

That's for ONE e-mail, a process that normally takes 15-20 seconds. And web pages load so slowly the glaciers will be completely melted before some of them appear. I have enough bandwidth that the up-and downloading I am doing should be almost unnoticeable. And I know it's something with the torrents because if I close down Ktorrent my internet connection goes back to normal. I don't even have to close it down -- just hitting the Stop button returns me to normal operation. What is sucking up my bandwidth?
>
> Probably the uploads, believe it or not.  TCP requires acknowledgments
> to operate correctly and includes a backoff mechanism when it detects
> congestion based on drops and retransmissions.  If your uploads are
> sucking up all your upstream bandwidth (which is less than downstream
> bandwidth for most broadband connections), then ACK messages will get
> lost/dropped, and congestion control will kick in.
>
> Bittorrent is a great concept, but unless you have a router that does
> quality of service traffic shaping, bittorrent uploads will definitely
> kill your outbound traffic speeds, and by doing so will also kill your
> download speeds.

I should add that your bittorrent client should have a mechanism to
rate limit upload speeds, so you can set it to something below your
upload speed for your broadband connection.  Remember to save room for
TCP/IP/Ethernet/etc. headers, so do something like 50%, which will
leave room for other traffic (i.e. email and http) and traffic
overhead like headers.

Jason

Thanks, that was a big help. Ktorrent's options are the following, together with what the defaults were when I opened the settings page ("0" is unlimited):

Queue manager
	Max downloads	0
	Maximum seeds	0
Preferences
	Max connections per torrent	0
	Global connection limit		0
	Maximum upload rate		0
	Maximum download rate	0
	Port						6881
	UDP tracker port			4444
	Number of upload slots		0
	Max share ratio			0

I changed the Maximum Upload Rate to 50 and it didn't make any difference. Then I dropped it to 25 and it made a big difference -- everything else was working much better, plus the download rate on F7-test2 went from an average of 25K/s up to 60+K/s. Finally, I set the maximum number of upload slots to 10 and the Maximum Upload Speed back to 50, and it seemed to be the same. Then I left the slots at 10 and dropped the upload speed back to 25. That was even better.

So now I guess I need to tweak these settings to maximize my own download while maintaining good torrent citizenship. Thanks for the pointers. Some day I may figure this all out.

wireless looked like it should work, but it couldn't find any networks.

ndsiwrapper. I'm sure if I actually installed F7 I could get the
wireless working wit ndiswrapper the same way I did in Edgy.

applications that came with it and everything worked except AbiWord,
which crashed on launching 100% of the time. Bad news: OpenOffice is
not on the CD. And you can't access any repositories so you can't
install anything, even though you have plenty of RAM to do so.

Bear in mind that this is the i386 version because they haven't
finished the amd64 live CD yet. So the 64-bit version may not work
quite as well. On the other hand, I think Feisty has some competition
on the way.

On 3/16/07, John Poelstra <poelcat at gmail.com> wrote:
>
> Jason Martin said the following on 03/15/2007 03:41 PM:
> > Probably the uploads, believe it or not.  TCP requires acknowledgments
> > to operate correctly and includes a backoff mechanism when it detects
> > congestion based on drops and retransmissions.  If your uploads are
> > sucking up all your upstream bandwidth (which is less than downstream
> > bandwidth for most broadband connections), then ACK messages will get
> > lost/dropped, and congestion control will kick in.
> >
> > Bittorrent is a great concept, but unless you have a router that does
> > quality of service traffic shaping, bittorrent uploads will definitely
> > kill your outbound traffic speeds, and by doing so will also kill your
> > download speeds.
>
> It sounds like a firewall or router problem.
>
> 1) What kind of router do you have?
> 2) Are you forwarding bittorrent ports to your host?
> 3) What type of firewall?
> 4) Which ISP?
> http://www.azureuswiki.com/index.php/Bad_ISPs


I have found two things that will cause the problems he is reporting.

1) if you are saturating the upload speed, download speed will drop on DSL.
Annoying, but true.  Seems to be just the way DSL works.

2) If your router has a small NAT buffer, it will get swamped and slow
down.  (BitTorrent tends to generate a huge number of connections.)
Sometimes a router update will fix this.  (If you have a Cisco home
router/firewall getting an update will fix this problem for the most part.)

Hope that helps.

	<c29537840703151541x6f67899du91eabe363c247c93@mail.gmail.com>
	<45FAD155.9030006@gmail.com>
	<55cf303a0703161106v41483b52kecfb56e2a59d727e@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <2e42bd9b0703161808p2793fe7et9567f578796ef773@mail.gmail.com>

On 3/16/07, Alan Olsen <alan.olsen at gmail.com> wrote:
> 1) if you are saturating the upload speed, download speed will drop on DSL.
> Annoying, but true.  Seems to be just the way DSL works.

As Jason, said this is due to the limiting of ACKs; the deep queuing
of DSL plus the fact that its usually asymmetric in favor of down
speeds means the ACKs just don't get there fast enough. I usually just
limit BT uploads to ~75-90% of my DSL upload capacity with good
results for interactive performance (read: ssh and web browsing) and
thus maintain marital bliss.

This might help too:
http://lartc.org/wondershaper/

-- 
Dan

is some banding in the colors, but that is typical of color lasers of its'

built, the Xerox should be a solid bet.

Subject: [PLUG] Any advise re color laser printer. Xerox 85xx? HP?

On 3/16/07, RParr <RParr at temporalarts.com> wrote:
> I am interested in replacing my old Lexmark Optra laser printer with a
> color laser printer.
>
> It's primary job is a low-to-moderate amount of standard office printing
> (duplex required) where reasonable cost per page is important.
>
> I wanted to go with color because I also do a fair amount of
> photographic/graphics printing but I do
> I have an Epson 2000P inkjet I use now use for that.
>
> I'd love tabloid size but that seems to bump the price way up.
>
> I was looking at the Xerox 8550/DN (when the rebates had them available
> for $499) or the newer 8560/DN which use the solid-ink technology.  They
> seem to have a nice clean design and the solid-ink *seems* like a good idea.
>
> I was wondering if anyone has experience with these models?
>
> I was also looking at the HP printers was worried I'd go broke buying
> replacement cartridges for them.  Any experience with these (especially
> the typical cost per page)

I recently bought an HP Color LaserJet 2605dn based on a previous
recommendation on this list.  It fits within your price range, thought
I haven't had it long enough to know about supply costs.  It isn't the
fastest thing around for black and white, but it is single pass so
color and black and white are about the same speed.  I think the only
thing I don't like about it so far is it chooses to re-calibrate
randomly, which is annoying in a home (I don't want it to recalibrate
in the middle of the night when I'm sleeping).

Feature-wise it has 10/100 networking, 64MB of memory, and a built-in
duplexer.  You can get a model that comes with an additional high
capacity tray for slightly more, but that wasn't necessary for our
use.  Compatible with my wife's XP machine, my Mac OS X, and the Linux
machine of whoever made the original recommendation.

Jason

> than this, it installed ok and things seem to be working fine.  Is this

> Desktop CD.  The laptop is an HP ze4300 with an ATI 320 mobile Radeon
> chipset.  In Windows, the function key to change the display output
> works normally.  I won't be able to respond quickly but suggestions will
> be read as soon as I have time.

> - m0gely


Hi, I'm new here and haven't posted before,  but here is my  2 bits  worth.
I used to have  a Sony  Viao  laptop  with  the same  apparent  problem.
Savage Virge chipset IIRC. The  fix for  me was  to  sneak  into  the
filesystem using  another  distro,  Puppy being  my  personal  favorite
rescue  system,  and edit  xorg.conf  to  read  "vesa"  for  the video
driver.  It  worked  every time.  Meaning  I'm  a distro  hopper.  It
occured  to  me  later  on  that  I  could  also  have  done  the  same
edit  B4  rebooting  into  the  installed  system,  but  don't  actually
recall  if  I  ever  tried  that.

Paul  Payne
Linux fanboy (quasi-geriatric) and geek wannabe since RH 5.2 was the new kid
on the block

In-Reply-To: <45FB23C6.2060908@TemporalArts.com>
References: <45FB23C6.2060908@TemporalArts.com>
Message-ID: <45FC2074.9000005@seitzassoc.com>

RParr wrote:
> I was looking at the Xerox 8550/DN (when the rebates had them available 
> for $499) or the newer 8560/DN which use the solid-ink technology.  They 
> seem to have a nice clean design and the solid-ink *seems* like a good 
> idea.

The solid ink printers make great prints, but in the past they have been
better suited to moderate to high volume environments.  The old units
that I have experience with would take a while to warm up, and waste some
ink in the process.  Thus it was better to leave them on continuously,
such as one might do in an office.  Today's printers have no doubt
improved.  If you are seriously considering solid ink, I could find 
out from how today's models perform. 

galen


From alan at clueserver.org  Sat Mar 17 11:32:25 2007
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Sat, 17 Mar 2007 11:32:25 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ANNOUNCEMENT: PLUG Advanced Topics Meeting March 19th 2007
Message-ID: <1174156345.28365.14.camel@zowie.fnordora.org>

PLUG Advanced Topics Meeting

March 19th, 2007 at 7pm

Jax Bar
826 SW 2nd Avenue Portland, Oregon

Speaker:   Randal Schwartz
           Stonehenge Consulting

Topic:     mod_perl

Abstract:	

What do sites like amazon.com, Ticketmaster, Citysearch, Slashdot, Wired
News, and PBS Online have in common?  They all run mod_perl behind the
scenes.  So does the Internet Movie Database, and almost everything
ending in "perl.org", and probably a lot more that you've visited
recently. mod_perl embeds the number one scripting language (Perl) into
the number one web server (Apache) to provide far more than just a quick
content delivery system... You can control and enhance every aspect of
the web delivery, from deciding which page to serve to whom, down to
logging interesting and unusual things.  Randal presents a brief
overview of mod_perl, including how to get it and what it can do for
you.

Happy Hour meal prices for the first hour.

On Mar 16, 2007, at 4:09 PM, RParr wrote:
>
> I was looking at the Xerox 8550/DN (when the rebates had them  
> available for $499) or the newer 8560/DN which use the solid-ink  
> technology.  They seem to have a nice clean design and the solid- 
> ink *seems* like a good idea.
>
> I was wondering if anyone has experience with these models?

I have used the 8550/DN at work and it is a great printer. I really  
like that the ink cartridges are blocks and a quick "lifting the  
hood" gives you visual confirmation of the levels. Each color slot  
holds 4 blocks of ink and it sure is nice how little space the spares  
take up. Galen is right about leaving the solid ink units on as they  
keep a pool of melted ink and have to melt it when cold starting.

Unfortunately, our main use required a switch to a laser so we now  
have the 8550/DN sitting idle. If you are still in the market,  
contact me off-list.

Keith


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Mar 17 17:24:15 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 17 Mar 2007 17:24:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Any advise re color laser printer.  Xerox 85xx? HP?
In-Reply-To: <45FC2074.9000005@seitzassoc.com>
References: <45FB23C6.2060908@TemporalArts.com>
	<45FC2074.9000005@seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <20070318002415.GA19235@gate.kl-ic.com>

> RParr wrote:
> >I was looking at the Xerox 8550/DN (when the rebates had them available 
> >for $499) or the newer 8560/DN which use the solid-ink technology.  They 
> >seem to have a nice clean design and the solid-ink *seems* like a good 
> >idea.

On Sat, Mar 17, 2007 at 10:08:04AM -0700, Galen Seitz wrote:
> The solid ink printers make great prints, but in the past they have been
> better suited to moderate to high volume environments.  The old units
> that I have experience with would take a while to warm up, and waste some
> ink in the process.  Thus it was better to leave them on continuously,
> such as one might do in an office.  Today's printers have no doubt
> improved.  If you are seriously considering solid ink, I could find 
> out from how today's models perform. 

I specifically *got rid of* a solid ink printer and replaced it with
an $500 hp2605dn (dn = duplexer, network) .  Yes, the supplies are
outrageous for the HP, and the toner cartridges are chipped, which
means that third-party toner usually won't work.  However, it is
Postscript (very important to me), prints beautifully, and is quiet
and low power - 17 watts idle, 300 watts printing ordinary paper,
perhaps 1KW printing labels and card stock.  It does recalibrate
from time to time, but that has never gotten in my way.  It goes
from idle to first print in seconds.

The device I got rid of was a Xerox Phaser 350 (an early model,
granted) for which I had a printer-and-a-half worth of spare parts,
and a lifetime supply of ink blocks from the Tektronix surplus store.
The damned thing would jam, misfeed paper, and streak.  I had one
printer head nozzle clog, requiring the replacement of the whole
fiendishly complicated and expensive head (hence -0.5 printer's worth
of spare parts).  If the power quit unexpectedly, it would sometimes
return to service flashing an error code that required opening covers
and turning the wheels inside.  I hope that the 8550 is designed better.

But what really soured me on the machine was the startup delay and
the power.  From a cold start, it required 10 minutes at 1000 watts
to heat the wax reservoir;  keeping the reservoir hot took 300 watts
continuously, while actually printing consumed 1000 watts.  After a
few hours, it goes into cold idle, necessitating another 10 minute
delay for the next print.  During the startup process, it calibrates
and cleans, and dumps about 5% of an ink block into the maintenance
tray while it cleans the pipes.  So if you did not have a lifetime
supply of ink blocks, the costs would mount up fast.  I figured it
was costing me $150 a year just to keep the damned thing ready to
print something.   Oh, and the wax-surface copies it made could not
be auto-fed through a copier.  Again, I hope the 8550 works better.

My ideal printer would be just like the hp2605dn (duplexer, network,
postscript, fast, low power, reliable) but with non-chipped toner
cartridges.  I imagine that some clever software hacker could make
my hp2605dn into my ideal printer by changing a few bytes of the
firmware, so it ignores the toner cartridge chips, and I hope
someone out there is thinking about how to do that. 

Keith

My Ubuntu amd64 Edgy laptop has a 16x CD/DVD+RW drive. I thought the
capacity of this drive was 4.7 GB on regular DVD+R media. I am trying
to burn 4.1 GB of data to a blank DVD on it. I have:

K3b
GnomeBaker
Nautilus
Bonfire

K3b announced that it could not burn one of the files (4.0 GB) because
the total was over 4.0 GB. 

GnomeBaker has a progress bar thingy to show how much the total files
that I selected will take up on the disk. The display showed plenty of
free space. It happily burned the entire thing to the DVD. When I
ejected and reinserted the DVD, the 3.9 GB file was missing. Thanks for
warning me.

Nautilus -- Ditto GnomeBaker, except it didn't have goodies like panel
icons showing the progress. And it wouldn't burn it faster than 2x.

Bonfire -- Ditto GnomeBaker.

Before I make any more coasters:

1) Is there a way to determine how much data I can put on a DVD in this
drive?

2) Is there any other DVD burning software besides the above?

On Sun, 18 Mar 2007 00:06:41 -0700
John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> dijo:

> My Ubuntu amd64 Edgy laptop has a 16x CD/DVD+RW drive. I thought the
> capacity of this drive was 4.7 GB on regular DVD+R media. I am trying
> to burn 4.1 GB of data to a blank DVD on it. 

I found the problem, but not the solution.

The problem is in mkisofs, which cannot handle a file greater than 4.0
GB. Googling, I found complaints from as long ago as 2002 that it could
not handle file sizes greater than 2.0 GB. Starting about 2004 the
complaints changed to 4.0 GB. It is now three years since the file size
limit was raised to 4.0 GB and evidently there is no fix yet. There are
also a lot of complaints that the directory structure was too deep for
it, although that was not my problem.

It may be that this is related to Joliet or something else in the
eldritch world of optical media filesystems.

Has anyone else experienced this and/or found a solution?

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Mar 18 10:11:06 2007

> Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> 
> >My ideal printer would be just like the hp2605dn (duplexer, network,
> >postscript, fast, low power, reliable) but with non-chipped toner
> >cartridges.  I imagine that some clever software hacker could make
> >my hp2605dn into my ideal printer by changing a few bytes of the
> >firmware, so it ignores the toner cartridge chips, and I hope
> >someone out there is thinking about how to do that. 

On Sun, Mar 18, 2007 at 09:37:28AM -0700, m0gely wrote:
> Or just look in the manual on how to override this setting.  It has to 
> be redone each time you put in a new cartridge.  But it's in there.  Not 
> all printers allow this, but that one does.

Indeed you are correct!  Page 134 of the User's Guide - "Cartridge
Out Override".  The printer will no longer estimate how full the
cartridges are, and you might run out of one of the colors while
printing, but one can estimate from the copy counter when a toner
refill will be needed.  So far, I've only used 10% of the toner, but
all the levels are dropping at approximately the same rate, which 
means that if one color runs out then all of them will be close to
needing a refill.

So, I guess this moves the hp2605dn printer from "good" to "ideal". 
Big grin.  Now I will talk all the rest of you into buying one, so
we can buy replacement toner in 55 gallon drums.  And if I can
replace the black toner cheaply, I may just retire my black-and-white
HP4M+, and use the 2605 for small black-and-white tasks, too.

Keith

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Thanks. I read the man page for growisofs, but it was kind of hard to
figure out. Evidently it is a front end for mkisofs.

The part I don't get is, assuming that growisofs is the solution, why
the GUIs (GnomeBaker, K3b, etc.) use only mkisofs. Growisofs has been
around for a couple years, so I would assume that it would be
incorporated into existing programs.

Oh well. I found a solution. I made the file into a .tar.gz which
brought it to just under 4.0 GB. K3b is happily writing it to DVD as I
write this. That gets me by for now, but the problem remains for the
future. And I was planning on building myself a desktop with a
double-layer drive. Wonder how people write to those?

Subject: Save the date! BarCamp Portland Tech Unconference, May 11-12

I am pleased to announce that BarCamp is coming to Portland on May 11-12! We 
will also be kicking off the regular DemoCamp event series during BarCamp to 
highlight tech startup activity in the Portland area.

Tech + Geek + Culture. The event for the Portland tech community, produced BY 
the Portland tech community.

What is BarCamp? It is an ad-hoc gathering born from the desire for people to 
share and learn in an open environment. It is an intense event with 
discussions, demos, and interaction from participants.

BarCamp is a FREE event and the content is determined by the attendees. The 
event will be hosted at CubeSpace, which has a number of conference rooms for 
breakout sessions, a large main meeting area, wireless access, easy access to 
public transportation, bike storage, and ample parking. Thanks to Eva and David 
at CubeSpace for signing up as space sponsors for the event (and to Ray at 
AboutUs for sponsoring the additional space costs).

We need your help to make BarCamp Portland a fantastic event for the tech 
community in Portland. Here's what you can do...

1) Forward this email on to people in the Portland area that may have an 
interest in attending. As we have done little marketing of the event (so far), 
assume that your local tech social network doesn't know about it yet.

2) If you have not already added yourself to the BarCamp Portland wiki page as 
an attendee, please do so. This will help us get a more accurate attendance 
count and plan accordingly (you want food, right?):

http://barcamp.org/BarCampPortland

3) Add a session idea for the event. This could be a talk, a demo, a roundtable 
discussions - whatever! Please add it to the Proposed Sessions section on the 
wiki page:

http://barcamp.org/BarCampPortland

4) Attend the BarCamp Portland Meetup this Thursday (03/22) evening 5:30-8pm at 
Jive Software downtown. Free beer on tap (thanks, Jive!), the opportunity to 
network with the tech community in Portland, and help plan for BarCamp 
Portland. More details:

http://barcamp.org/BarCampPortlandMeetups

5) Help identify sponsors. CubeSpace and AboutUs are already onboard as 
sponsors for the space and related costs (and Jive Software has been our 
ongoing meetup and planning sponsor). We are looking for sponsors to cover 
food, drinks, and t-shirts.

I hope to see you this Thursday evening at Jive Software for our monthly tech 
meetup and BarCamp Portland planning meeting!

PS: If this isn't your type of thing and you want off my BarCamp Portland 
mailing list, please reply to this email. I will only do a few mailings before 
the event in May (and this list will never be used for commercial purposes).

Rich,

If I am understanding your question correctly, it seems that the java
executable is not in your $PATH. I am not sure where your install put things
but you should be able to do a locate (if you have run an updatedb recently)
or just do a find. 

You can either use the fully qualified path to the executable or add the
java bin directory to your PATH variable in your profile.

Doug

Well, yes it is.  That's always been good enough for my purposes. It seems
likely your card is capable of better performance, but I've never had one
like it.  My current laptop uses the ATI Radeon Express 200M, and it seems
pretty smooth.  PCLinuxOS 2007 is my distro of choice for now, and it is
using the "ati" driver .  Did your xorg.conf use the "ati" driver
originally?  It's possible you may get what you want using a Modeline in
your "Monitor" section.

Mine is : ModeLine "1280x800_50"  68.56  1280 1336 1472 1664  800 801 804
824  -HSync +Vsync  Try that at your own risk, since I don't know what a
wildly incorrect modeline will do to an LCD screen.  Most likely nothing,
but maybe someone else knows for sure.

That's on a lapop with a 15.4" WXGA Screen.  I've never seen the _50 in
modeline before.  No idea what it does.
OpenSuse 10.2 and Ubuntu 6.10 also work fine with this hardware.  Sound is
another issue, however.  I hope someone with more expertise can jump in
here.  I've reached the limit of my knowledge.

built three types of boxes, depending upon the specs:  FreekBoxes for
the volunteers, boxes to be donated to other non-profits, and boxes to
sell in the store.  AFAIK, there's no support available specifically for
boxes people purchase from the store, but there's no official support
for FreekBoxes either.  It's pretty much a community-based effort,
meaning if you need support you can ask around for help and make use of
whatever resources Free Geek has.  And of course, there's the PLUG
Clinic for support.

But if you mean, is there an 800# to call?, I don't think so.  All boxes
built there are tested, meaning they should be in working order when you
take them home.  Beyond that, it's a kind of DIY thing.

That's all to the best of my recollection, anyway.


-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA

of absolute reality; even larks and katydids are supposed, by some, to
dream." --S. Jackson



From johnxj at comcast.net  Mon Mar 19 09:29:33 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Mon, 19 Mar 2007 09:29:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Partition confusion
Message-ID: <20070319092933.cfffdac2.johnxj@comcast.net>

I have a 160 GB external USB drive. Until now it has been hanging on my
Windows 2000 desktop. When I acquired the drive over a year ago I used
the disk utility in Windows 2000 to create two NTFS partitions of ~75
GB. I have decided I want to move it back and forth between the Windows
desktop and my Linux laptop, each of which would have a partition it
could recognize to use for backups. Therefore, I want to reformat the
second partition as ext3.

This morning I moved it to the Linux laptop, where gparted recognized
it as /dev/sdf and said it had 1019 MiB unallocated. Gparted saw no
other partitions. I googled, and apparently MiB are mebibytes, which is
close to the same thing as a megabyte, so gparted was saying there was
about 1 GB unallocated. I'll leave the issue of why we need a new term
that no one understands for a later philosophical discussion.

Figuring that the 1 GB unallocated was just some left over space, I
moved it back to the Windows 2000 desktop and deleted the second
partition. Then I brought it back to the laptop, where gparted still
says there is 1019 MiB unallocated. Gparted is not seeing the remaining
NTFS partition, and is lying about the unallocated space. However, I
selected the 1019 MiB and clicked on New, to see what would happen if I
tried to create a new disk label. Gparted said that a new disk label
was required in order to create a partition and that setting one would
delete all data from /dev/sdf. This isn't making any sense to me at
all. I don't care if gparted and Linux can't see the NTFS partition,
but how can I get it to recognize the correct free space so I can
create a partition on it and format it ext3?


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Mar 19 09:41:54 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 19 Mar 2007 09:41:54 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Special Topics Workshops for PLUG?
Message-ID: <20070319164154.GB17309@gate.kl-ic.com>


PLUG has three monthly gatherings - the general meeting, advanced 
topics, and linux clinic.  I propose a fourth meeting, scheduled
as the need appears and when an organizer volunteers:

Special Topics Workshop

The special topics workshop is for installing and configuring
specific pieces of software.  Examples might include  VMware,
Mediawiki, Samba, ndiswrapper or video codecs.  The workshop would
follow a format that would be a blend of Advanced Topics and Linux
Clinic; perhaps an hour devoted to a lecture/demonstration, followed
by an hour of install and an hour of configuration and testing.
Attendees are be expected to read up in advance, look onlines for 
potential problems with their particular distro and setup,  and
download prerequisites and dependencies if possible, then bring
their machines and be ready to work.  Each of the workshops would
need an organizer to arrange for experts to be there, and perhaps
a facilitator to arrange the facility in advance.  The setup we
use for Linux Clinic at Free Geek might work, but that depends now
on having Rick babysit us (thanks, Rick!) as a representative of
Free Geek.  We could also just devote particular clinics to these
topics, but folks wanting to work on general problems would have
to wait for the next month.

This should be a "demand-pull" arrangement;  individuals wanting
to learn about a particular topic will have to do a lot of legwork
to get a workshop set up.  Because not many PLUG members find the
time to volunteer, this might not work out, or happen only rarely. 
On the other hand, this might appeal to some of you, and be the
way you want to give back to PLUG.  Ideas?

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs


From robert.citek at gmail.com  Mon Mar 19 09:47:46 2007
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Mon, 19 Mar 2007 11:47:46 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] Partition confusion
In-Reply-To: <20070319092933.cfffdac2.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070319092933.cfffdac2.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <45FEBEB2.6080106@gmail.com>

John Jason Jordan wrote:
> However, I selected the 1019 MiB and clicked on New, to see what
> would happen if I tried to create a new disk label. Gparted said that
> a new disk label was required in order to create a partition and that
> setting one would delete all data from /dev/sdf. This isn't making
> any sense to me at all. I don't care if gparted and Linux can't see
> the NTFS partition, but how can I get it to recognize the correct
> free space so I can create a partition on it and format it ext3?

Can you plug the external drive into your linux machine, run this
script, and then post the contents of output.txt:

true ; ( set -x
cat /etc/issue.net
uname -a
cat /proc/partitions
fdisk -l
true ) >& output.txt

The above script should give a clearer picture of what your drive looks
like.

Regards,
- Robert

From griffint at pobox.com  Mon Mar 19 11:26:43 2007
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)

>
> PLUG has three monthly gatherings - the general meeting, advanced
> topics, and linux clinic.  I propose a fourth meeting, scheduled
> as the need appears and when an organizer volunteers:
>
> Special Topics Workshop
>
> The special topics workshop is for installing and configuring
> specific pieces of software.  Examples might include  VMware,
> Mediawiki, Samba, ndiswrapper or video codecs.
> [snip]

This sort idea has crossed my mind several times. In my head I was
calling this new type of gathering "PLUG Classroom". It would be
organized like typical technical instruction with a mix of lecture
and lab time. It would meet at most once a month but not necessarily
every month.

To be practical it would require a three or four hour block of time,
which likely means on the weekend. Classroom-ish facilities would be
helpful.

When classroom instruction fails its usually because of lack of
preparation by the instructor or the students. To help the students
its important to get the prerequisites published well in advance.
This should include knowledge the student is expected to have, and
the requirements for the student's computer (installed s/w etc.)
in cases where they are bringing their own computers.

Terry




From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Mar 19 12:20:48 2007

Subject: [PLUG] Freegeek computers and support?
In-Reply-To: <45FC054C.7040205@gmail.com>

Message-ID: <20070319192048.GA19186@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Sat, Mar 17, 2007 at 10:12:12AM -0500, Robert Citek wrote:
> 
> I sent these questions to {info,sales}@freegeek.org, but thought people
> here might know the answer,too.
> 
> I noticed that one can obtain a Linux computer (FreekBox) by either
> volunteering for the Build Program or the Adopt a Computer program.  Can
> one also purchase a FreekBox?  If so, do they come with support?

You can buy a StoreBox at a decent price ($50 to $150).  There is
no support.  Some folks drag in their store/freek boxes to the Linux
Clinic and we help them there, sometimes.  Others ask on mailing
lists.  There are organizations that provide professional support
for $$$, but that support will be cheaper if you buy the machines
and run the software they suggest.

Support requires human attention, and that is rarely free.  We help
each other out on this mailing list;  that means we give each other
more than we get, though a few people here haven't figured that out
yet. :-/   Help us, and we are more likely to help you.

Keith

I must say that I am simply overwhelmed by the whole Freegeeks concept.  
It is the Habitat For Humanity of the cyberworld, and I just wish I was 
more adept and proficient at both the hardware and software end of 
things, so that I could make a more substantial contribution to the 
whole enterprise.

Most people don't realize just how life-altering a modest computer and 
an internet connection can be for a kid starting out or a family on the 
margin.  Thanks to the work Freegeeks does, every motivated young person 
in the Portland metropolitan area has the opportunity to acquire his own 
computer.

When you think about it, it is simply amazing. You guys can't be thanked 
enough.

VMWare is a product that emulates an (x86-based, afaik) computer in
software, so you can run multiple OSes simultaneously.

This brings up something that's been gnawing at me for a while... I
was out of the hardware/tech loop for a while, and I don't know where
to start with the new Virtualization "stuff" that's available.  I
don't know if that would be a good topic for a workshop (maybe an
advanced topics meeting?) but it's something I'd love to have a
tutorial on :)

From tonyr42 at gmail.com  Mon Mar 19 16:19:45 2007
From: tonyr42 at gmail.com (Tony Rick)
Date: Mon, 19 Mar 2007 16:19:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Partition confusion
In-Reply-To: <20070319092933.cfffdac2.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070319092933.cfffdac2.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4da3d49b0703191619j16cd7834s952deffbc4c124cb@mail.gmail.com>

On 3/19/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
>
>
> Figuring that the 1 GB unallocated was just some left over space, I
> moved it back to the Windows 2000 desktop and deleted the second
> partition. Then I brought it back to the laptop, where gparted still
> says there is 1019 MiB unallocated. Gparted is not seeing the remaining
> NTFS partition, and is lying about the unallocated space. However, I
> selected the 1019 MiB and clicked on New, to see what would happen if I
> tried to create a new disk label. Gparted said that a new disk label
> was required in order to create a partition and that setting one would
> delete all data from /dev/sdf. This isn't making any sense to me at
> all. I don't care if gparted and Linux can't see the NTFS partition,
> but how can I get it to recognize the correct free space so I can
> create a partition on it and format it ext3?
>

To libparted (the library for which gparted is a front end), 'disk label'
means
'partition table'.  You were directing it to create a new partition
table, and so to
wipe out all previous partition definitions.  I don't know why gparted is
confused
about what partitions are or are not defined.  Robert suggested 'sudo fdisk
-l /dev/sdf'
to list the partitions.  You could also try 'sudo parted /dev/sdf print' to
see what
libparted thinks of the partition table.  (parted is a cli front end to
libparted, not
otherwise related to gparted, afaik).

- tony

directory you were in when you ran it -- everything of interest will
be in there.

( the >& output.txt part of the script is the indicator -- ">&" takes
everything that would normally show up on screen and puts into
whatever comes next, in this case, the file output.txt)

From sean.whitney at gmail.com  Mon Mar 19 17:39:46 2007
From: sean.whitney at gmail.com (Sean Whitney)
Date: Mon, 19 Mar 2007 17:39:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] C++ question
Message-ID: <45FF2D52.2010004@gmail.com>

I'm working my way through an older C++ programming book and have found
an error that I can't explain with what the book is expressing.

This program:

#include <iostream>

using namespace std;

int main()
{
 int localVariable = 5;
 int * pLocal = &localVariable;
 int * pHeap = new int;
 if (pHeap == NULL)
    {
        cout << "Error! No memory for pHeap!\n";
    }
    *pHeap = 7;
    cout << "localVariable: " << localVariable << "\n";
    cout << "*pLocal: " << *pLocal << "\n";
    cout << "*pHeap: "  << *pHeap  << "\n";
    delete pHeap;
    if (pHeap == NULL)
       {
           cout << "Error! No memory for pHeap!\n";
        }
    *pHeap = 9;
    cout << "*pHeap: " << *pHeap << "\n";
    delete pHeap;

}

is supposed to be displaying how to delete pointers using delete,
wherein it will release the free memory, but the pointer *pHeap is still
local and as such still exists and can be reassigned a new free memory
location.  However under g++ anytime both delete statements are present,
the program will compile but produce the following error.

localVariable: 5
*pLocal: 5
*pHeap: 7
*pHeap: 9
*** glibc detected *** double free or corruption (fasttop): 0x0804a008 ***
Aborted

Commenting out either delete statements removes the error.

I have discovered that C++ has changed a bit since the book was written,
so I've made a few modifications to the program (namely using using
namespace std; and int main () instead of void main()).  Anyone care to
explain why error occurs?


Sean

<i> </i>

From jason at jasonjustman.com  Mon Mar 19 18:49:15 2007
From: jason at jasonjustman.com (jason justman)
Date: Mon, 19 Mar 2007 18:49:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] C++ question
In-Reply-To: <45FF2D52.2010004@gmail.com>
References: <45FF2D52.2010004@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <45FF3D9B.1000103@jasonjustman.com>

Hmm, it looks like a typo of some sort to me - an introductory book on 
c++ probably isn't trying to show you potental buffer/doublefree bugs.  
You've lucked out, because usually making an assignment to a * that has 
been deleted might cause a segfault.

In general, calling delete on a * that has already been deleted is a bad 
thing - glibc has detected this and is trying to protect you from 
yourself - http://www.owasp.org/index.php/Double_Free


Having said all that, i'm almost 100% sure you are missing the following 
inbetween:

delete pHeap;
if (pHeap == NULL)


 pHeap = new int;

The author is probably trying to show you how to delete and create a new 
*, rather than segmentation faults in your program.

Thanks,
J


Sean Whitney wrote:
> I'm working my way through an older C++ programming book and have found
> an error that I can't explain with what the book is expressing.
>

>

>     cout << "*pHeap: "  << *pHeap  << "\n";

>        {
>            cout << "Error! No memory for pHeap!\n";

>

> Here is the contents of output.txt:

Good info.  Something strange is happening, especially since linux sees
the drive as 1 GB and you claim it's 160 GB.

Try running the two scripts below as root.  To become root using Ubuntu,
type "sudo su -" and then enter your password.  I'm assuming you have
admin priviledges.  Make sure the external drive is unplugged before you
run the first script.  In fact, it may be best to power down, remove the
drive, and then power on.

# script 1:
dmesg -c | tail

Now plug in your drive and type this script:

# script 2:
true ; ( set -x
dmesg
fdisk -l
true ) >& output.txt

Please post the contents of output.txt.

From fpikus at gmail.com  Mon Mar 19 23:39:00 2007
From: fpikus at gmail.com (Fedor Pikus)

You're missing one line in the code (if you copied it accurately, then
the book is missing it):

On 3/19/07, Sean Whitney <sean.whitney at gmail.com> wrote:
> I'm working my way through an older C++ programming book and have found
> an error that I can't explain with what the book is expressing.
>
> This program:
>
> #include <iostream>
>
> using namespace std;
>
> int main()
> {
>  int localVariable = 5;
>  int * pLocal = &localVariable;
>  int * pHeap = new int;
>  if (pHeap == NULL)
>     {
>         cout << "Error! No memory for pHeap!\n";
>     }
>     *pHeap = 7;
>     cout << "localVariable: " << localVariable << "\n";
>     cout << "*pLocal: " << *pLocal << "\n";
>     cout << "*pHeap: "  << *pHeap  << "\n";
>     delete pHeap;

Here we should have:
pHeap = new int;
>     if (pHeap == NULL)
>        {
>            cout << "Error! No memory for pHeap!\n";
>         }

Without the line above there is no reason to check for NULL because
pHeap was not reassigned, if it wasn't NULL before it still isn't.
With that line, the new expression can return NULL pointer if there is
not enough memory (well, the standard-compliant new will throw
std::bad_alloc exception but when the book was written it was probably
not the case).

>     *pHeap = 9;
>     cout << "*pHeap: " << *pHeap << "\n";
>     delete pHeap;
>
> }
>
> is supposed to be displaying how to delete pointers using delete,
> wherein it will release the free memory, but the pointer *pHeap is still
> local and as such still exists and can be reassigned a new free memory
> location.  However under g++ anytime both delete statements are present,
> the program will compile but produce the following error.
>
> localVariable: 5
> *pLocal: 5
> *pHeap: 7
> *pHeap: 9
> *** glibc detected *** double free or corruption (fasttop): 0x0804a008 ***
> Aborted
>
> Commenting out either delete statements removes the error.
>
> I have discovered that C++ has changed a bit since the book was written,
> so I've made a few modifications to the program (namely using using
> namespace std; and int main () instead of void main()).  Anyone care to
> explain why error occurs?
>

On Mar 19, 2007, at 12:20 PM, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> We help
> each other out on this mailing list;  that means we give each other
> more than we get, though a few people here haven't figured that out
> yet. :-/

Something I figured out working with the PHP project was that the  
vast number of "information leechers" really didn't know how to be  
useful, yet, and the vast number of greybeards were often tired of  
repeating themselves. The middle-ware, the middle tier, is the folks  
who are frequently active, can bridge reading archives and teaching  
newbies, and are really good at understanding both guru *and*  
apprentice work.

It's not always a common set of skills, and (IMNSHO) the only way to  
really learn "it" (or I.T., for that matter?)  is to become a humble  
apprentice, and masterful guru, at the same time. And clap with one  
hand, of course. :)

(Masters who are not apprentices need not apply.)

-Ronabop

now able to use Ubuntu in accelerated mode on my laptop.  I don't have 
the page handy or my xorg.conf file available, but if anyone is 
interested let me know and I will post those tidbits.

From aaron at bavariati.org  Tue Mar 20 09:06:30 2007
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Tue, 20 Mar 2007 09:06:30 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Freegeek computers and support?
In-Reply-To: <45FC054C.7040205@gmail.com>
References: <45FC054C.7040205@gmail.com>

On Sat, Mar 17, 2007 at 10:12:12AM -0500, Robert Citek wrote:
> Hello all,
> 
> I sent these questions to {info,sales}@freegeek.org, but thought people
> here might know the answer,too.
> 
> I noticed that one can obtain a Linux computer (FreekBox) by either
> volunteering for the Build Program or the Adopt a Computer program.  Can
> one also purchase a FreekBox?  If so, do they come with support?

At the store, you can get a computer that's a little lower-spec than a
Freekbox for $25-$70 or one that's quite a bit nicer for $150-$250.

You can also buy laptops sometimes.  Thursdays are best for that.

The computers come with a coupon for a single support incident.  I think
you can buy more coupons if you want.  If you install Windows, you're on
your own.

You can get a 50% discount on store purchases if you volunteer for a few
hours.  There's always things to wash or stuff to recycle.

Store hours are 11-7 Tue-Sat.

On Tue, Mar 20, 2007 at 12:12:30AM -0700, Michael M. wrote:
> A nice idea, though for me it has the same problem as the clinic:  no
> means of transporting a machine to the location.  I suppose one day I'll
> get a laptop, but I don't really need one now.

A problem for many, no doubt. 

If your machine is accessable over a reasonably fast VPN, we can do
almost everything that we can if your machine is on-site at the clinic
(we can bring up a desktop remotely, for example).   If your machine 
is accessable by SSH, and has yum or apt set up, we can set up a VPN.

If your machine is not accessable, perhaps we can agree on some kind
of swap tray infrastructure, so that you merely bring in the hard 
drive.  That could be your project for PLUG - researching the swap
tray options, and convincing other PLUG members and Free Geek to
use that kind of tray in the accessable machines, and buying a few
for yourself and for Free Geek.  I will be easier to convince if you
use the ViPower Parallel ATA133 tray, or alternately the InClose tray.
The ViPower trays are available at ENU, and also fairly inexpensively
by mail order, while the InClose trays are available at Frys.  The
ViPower are slightly more reliable, slightly cheaper, and have both
slide-switch and key-access enclosures, so the Free Geek machines
could be keyed.

This would be a lot of work, and might cost as much as two taxi trips
to move your hardware to one clinic day.  But it would create a
system that could be used many times, and would help a lot of others.

Keith

it on our main mail/web server at work. So far, everything feels 
pretty solid. There are two big jumps for me:

  * mbox (UW imapd) -> maildir (Dovecot)
  * Apache 2.0 -> 2.2

There have supposedly been some SELinux enhancements between CentOS 4 
and 5, but I probably won't get bitten until I try integrating my 
SELinux problem children: Trac and its related Subversion 
repositories, and Tomcat.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue Mar 20 13:02:50 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 20 Mar 2007 13:02:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Whitebox to CentOS (Consider Scientific Linux)
In-Reply-To: <004901c76b0f$edcdafb0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
References: <004901c76b0f$edcdafb0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
Message-ID: <20070320200250.GA580@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Tue, Mar 20, 2007 at 09:50:53AM -0700, William A Morita wrote:
> I saw some discussion a while back about CentOS being a better
> maintained version of RHEL than Whitebox.
> Has anyone out there done a Whitebox --> CentOS conversion?
> If so, how did it go?  Any pitfalls to be on the lookout for?

CentOS is a very well maintained clone of Red Hat Enterprise Linux.
You should also take a look at Scientific Linux, which is Yet
Another Clone of RHEL.  The avantage of SL is that it is supported
for a Very Long Time by some full time professionals at Fermilab. 
You will have access to all the RHEL/CentOS updates while those are
available, of course, but you will also continue to get upgrades
after RHEL and CentOS have reached End-Of-Life.  The SL folks have
promised a minimum of 5 year support, but the maximum is for as
long as there is funding for Fermilab and other science labs.  


and other apps common in science academia.  Most of the big 
supercomputing arrays use SL.  This is the reason why they want very
long term support;  they don't want to redesign apps for computing
arrays or for experiment controllers.  Other than that, the
differences between SL and CentOS are quite small, and if you can
handle the shorter (but still long) support horizon for CentOS, then
CentOS might be the best bet because of the more active community. 

All RHEL clones have much longer life than Fedora, though not as
many bleeding edge goodies.  All are "Redhat-Like", meaning things
are sometimes stored in different places than Debian stores them.
Changing from Debian (Ubuntu, Knoppix) to Redhat, or to SUSE, or
to other major variants is more pain than most people want.

RHEL5, based mostly on FC6, has just been released.  CentOS 5
and SL 5 will follow soon.  I played a bit with RHEL beta 2.  As
always, some things are new and wonderful, some things are upgraded
and better, and some things are broken for the sake of perversity.
Before you upgrade, do backups first!

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Subject: [PLUG] What program is using a library?
Message-ID: <20070320193145.4081f511.johnxj@comcast.net>

Today Ubuntu tells me that I have 25 updates available. The majority
have to do with something called aqbanking. At one time I tried GnuCash
and another time I had Kmymoney installed, but I have done a complete
removal of both a long time ago. The only accounting program I use is
Quickbooks, and that is running under Crossover Office. I think I
should just remove all these things, but first I need to figure out
what installed them. I know I read somewhere how you can find out what
package installed a library, but I can't find the secret command. Does
anyone know?


From wamorita at hevanet.com  Tue Mar 20 20:02:05 2007
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Tue, 20 Mar 2007 20:02:05 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] What program is using a library?
In-Reply-To: <20070320193145.4081f511.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070320193145.4081f511.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <000c01c76b65$4fa7e2a0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>

Jason,

You may have removed the packages, but the libraries that were loaded to
support those packages do not automatically get removed.

You can find out what libraries you have loaded that are no longer
referenced by programs using "deborphan".

To remove these packages use:
	apt-get =y --force-yes --purge remove $(deborphan)

After running, run deborphan again to see if there are secondary references
that are not longer needed.
Use the apt-get command above as many times as needed to get to a state
where deborphan returns no libraries.

I suggest doing this after removing programs.

- Bill Morita
wamorita at hevanet.com

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Tue Mar 20 20:20:40 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Tue, 20 Mar 2007 20:20:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] What program is using a library?
In-Reply-To: <20070320193145.4081f511.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070320193145.4081f511.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <200703202020.40473.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from John Jason Jordan
# on Tuesday 20 March 2007 07:31 pm:

>Ubuntu...

> think I should just remove all these things, but first I need to
> figure out what installed them. I know I read somewhere how you can
> find out what package installed a library, but I can't find the
> secret command. Does anyone know?

If you just remove what you don't want, the package manager should tell 
you what else is going to be removed with it.  If you don't want to get 
rid of everything that comes up in the list, you need it.

If your gui is in the way, try `konqueror man:aptitude` and search for 
"don't".  You could probably batch-up something with a '-s -y' and 
piping to grep ala:

  aptitude remove python -s -y | grep -A 2 'REMOVED:$' | grep '^ '

Though that '-A 2' is pretty naive, so roughly:

  aptitude remove python -s -y | perl -e 'while(<>) {chomp;
    if(/^The following/) {$ok = /REMOVED:$/; next;}
    $ok and print "$_\n";}'

There might be something builtin for that, like maybe apt-cache rdepends 
or so.  I think "man:apt-cache" works in konqueror too.

`strace -o /tmp/stracefile $program_name ; grep '\.so' /tmp/stracefile` 
will tell you at what run-time libraries are being loaded (and a huge 
lot of other info), but I don't think that's what you want unless 
you're trying to fit mozilla into a shoebox and wondering why ftp links 
quit working when you tossed the .jar files.

--Eric
-- 
Introducing change is like pulling off a bandage: the pain is a memory
almost as soon as you feel it.
--Paul Graham

    http://scratchcomputing.com

Subject: [PLUG] What program is using a library?
In-Reply-To: <000c01c76b65$4fa7e2a0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
References: <20070320193145.4081f511.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<000c01c76b65$4fa7e2a0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
Message-ID: <20070320202201.53280e60.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Tue, 20 Mar 2007 20:02:05 -0700
"William A Morita" <wamorita at hevanet.com> dijo:

> Jason,
> 
> You may have removed the packages, but the libraries that were loaded to
> support those packages do not automatically get removed.
> 
> You can find out what libraries you have loaded that are no longer
> referenced by programs using "deborphan".
> 
> To remove these packages use:
> 	apt-get =y --force-yes --purge remove $(deborphan)
> 
> After running, run deborphan again to see if there are secondary references
> that are not longer needed.
> Use the apt-get command above as many times as needed to get to a state
> where deborphan returns no libraries.
> 
> I suggest doing this after removing programs.

Bill,

That sounds like exactly what I need!

Unfortunately, "deborphan" got me "command not found," and a search in
Synaptic revealed that it exists but was not installed. The search also
popped up gtkdeborphan, which is a GUI front end for it. I marked them
for installation and then discovered that the Ubuntu repositories seem
to be down. :(

Actually, Synaptic does a pretty good job of listing orphaned stuff, If
you click on the Status button it will show you things that are
"residual - auto removable." Unfortunately, sometimes it doesn't work.
I think where it fails is when you have installed a program and then
you install a plugin or extension or something for it. Synaptic doesn't
know that the additional stuff is connected to another package, so if
you remove the package the extra stuff gets left behind. At least
that's my theory.

I also googled on the mysterious aqbanking stuff and it turns out that
it is somehow connected to GnuCash. I just did a complete removal on
all of them. So far everything on my little computer remains happy.

Well, the Ubuntu repositories finally came back and I installed
deborphan and gtkorphan. I used gtkorphan because it's easier. It
listed 41 orphaned packages, but I uninstalled only seven of them. The
problem is that I can't tell if something might actually need it. For
example, one of them was libtar. Gtkorphan lets you right-click and get
information about the package. Among other things, for libtar it said:

Description: C library for manipulating tar archives
 libtar allows programs to create, extract and test tar archives.
 It supports both the strict POSIX tar format and many of the
commonly-used GNU extensions.

So if I remove it, what will I break? I don't know the answer to that,
so I decided to leave it. And the others are all equally confusing. 

Deborphan is a good tool, but I need a database or something where I
can look up a library and see what packages use it.

Subject: [PLUG] Serial Port issue
Message-ID: <46017E11.3000400@comcast.net>

At the clinic on Sunday, Aaron helped me compile a program that
communicates with a device that connects to a serial port on my machine.
The device is a controller for the X-10 system of plug-in modules (to
the house wiring). We succeeded in compiling and running the program and
running it as root. We also added rsteff to the uucp group (I think I
have that right) so that I'd be able to run the program without having
to have root privileges.

I now have some time to work with the program again, but find that it
does not respond the way it did on Sunday afternoon, either as rsteff or
as root. One of the commands, x10 info, returns the current settings of
the device. On Sunday it did return those settings. Today nothing
appears to happen. Upon typing the command ./x10 info, the cursor moves
to the next line and sits there until I type <ctrl>c. As I said, it does
the same thing whether I'm rsteff or root. The X-10 controller has
default settings so that I can operate devices A01 through A08. These
work as expected.

Where do I start to look for what to do to solve this?

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Mar 21 11:53:57 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Wed, 21 Mar 2007 11:53:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Special Topics Workshops for PLUG?
In-Reply-To: <20070319164154.GB17309@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20070319164154.GB17309@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <20070321115357.c49d9116.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Mon, 19 Mar 2007 09:41:54 -0700
Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> dijo:

> Special Topics Workshop

I have read the excellent suggestions from others, and have two to add:

Potential topic: 
A backup how-to for newbie desktop users. The majority on this list
work in a situation where daily backups of servers need to be made,
including off-site storage, disaster recovery, etc. That is certainly
an important topic (recently covered at a fine PLUG monthly meeting),
but there is a completely different dimension to backups. I'm thinking
about the person who has just one computer at home. This person uses it
to surf, write an occasional letter, and probably does some media stuff
-- editing toonz or vids, etc. This person does not need or want daily
backups to tape with off-site storage and disaster recovery plans. This
person does need a plan for a hard disk crash and, probably more
commonly, recovering a document that was inadvertently deleted. It is
something that needs to be done on an "as needed" basis. There is
little in the literature that can help this user, and the few software
packages that are available are buggy or useless. Training in dirvish,
rsync, tar, along with hands-on tweaking of the options available in
each of these tools is what the newbie desktop user should have.

A general issue:
Would it be possible to record the proceedings of these special topics
workshops somehow? I am thinking of a podcast or something which can be
made available on the PLUG website.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Mar 21 20:58:29 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Wed, 21 Mar 2007 20:58:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Exasperation
Message-ID: <20070321205829.733c46a2.johnxj@comcast.net>

I decided it was time to build myself a new desktop. I wanted to use it
for backups as well, so I decided to put a couple of 320 GB SATA II
drives in it with Raid 1. A couple hours online and a couple more at
Fry's, and I'm back home with all the goodies. Everything, that is,
except the monitor. Fry's monitor selectiion sucks. I decided that for
the time being I'd just use an old professional 17" CRT that I had left
over. Back at home, a couple hours later I have it all put together. 

Nothing comes up on the screen. Pulling stuff out, re-reading the
manual, double-checking, still nothing comes up on the screen. Finally
I decided to try the CRT hooked up to the Windows desktop, just to see
if it's the monitor. Voil?! But grrrr. All that double-checking and it
was the monitor the whole time.

So I decided I'd just buzz up to Jantzen Beach and get as nice flat
panel widescreen at Circuit City or CompUSA. I start the car, turn on
the lights, and no headlights. Just parking lights. At this point I am
starting to swear in Spanish.

OK, if neither headlight comes on, clearly it's a fuse. Ford actually
designed the fuse panel properly in this vehicle -- it's right at the
left of the dash and you can reach it without crawling around. So I
pulled on the little tab to open it up and -- it's stuck. I can't get
the panel open. And it's dark. And the store will be closing in 45
minutes. Hijo ... well, never mind.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Thu Mar 22 19:43:32 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Thu, 22 Mar 2007 19:43:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] BIOS, Raid 1 and Confusion
Message-ID: <20070322194332.d79c2f4e.johnxj@comcast.net>

The motherboard in my shiny new computer supports Raid 0 and Raid 1. I
went into the BIOS and set it to Raid 1, which it said was "mirroring"
for the two 320 GB SATA II drives, both of which were visible in the
BIOS settings.

I expected that from that point onward all software would see it as one
320 GB drive, i.e., that the BIOS would handle the mirroring. Or the
onboard SATA II controller. Or something.

But when I go to install Linux the partitioning utility sees two
separate 320 GB hard disks. This happened for OpenSuse 10.2,
Mandriva-free-2007 and Ubuntu Feisty, all 64-bit versions.

I aborted each installation and am sitting here trying to figure out
what is happening, what I did wrong, and/or what I have misunderstood.

Does anyone have any suggestions for what I am not grasping here?


From danielmyoung at gmail.com  Thu Mar 22 21:14:53 2007
From: danielmyoung at gmail.com (Dan Young)
Date: Thu, 22 Mar 2007 21:14:53 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] BIOS, Raid 1 and Confusion
In-Reply-To: <20070322194332.d79c2f4e.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070322194332.d79c2f4e.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <2e42bd9b0703222114k5dff6a53k526849979a99ed79@mail.gmail.com>

On 3/22/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> The motherboard in my shiny new computer supports Raid 0 and Raid 1. I
> went into the BIOS and set it to Raid 1, which it said was "mirroring"
> for the two 320 GB SATA II drives, both of which were visible in the
> BIOS settings.

The RAID on most consumer motherboards is colloquially known as
"fakeraid" and is very likely to not work in non-Windows operating
systems.

Do an "lspci" form the live CD then go here for the full skinny on
your particular hardware.
http://linuxmafia.com/faq/Hardware/sata.html
http://linuxmafia.com/faq/Hardware/sata.html#fakeraid

-- 
Dan

OpenSUSE 10.2
Mandriva-free-2007
Ubuntu Feisty
Ubuntu Edgy
Ubuntu Dapper

The first thing I did was go back into the BIOS and read more
carefully. After poking around a bit I finally managed to make the two
320 GB SATA II drives into a Raid 1 array (mirroring). Then I started
trying to install the above distros. Dapper couldn't even start x on
the live DVD. Edgy got x started, but couldn't find the Raid 1 array.
It still saw two 320 GB drives. I told it to go ahead and install on
sda1 (these drives show up as sda and sdb instead of hda and hdb). It
blew up trying to read the disks. Feisty got further -- it still
couldn't see the Raid 1 array, but when I told it to go ahead and
install on sda it got to the point where it started installing before
crashing. Mandriva did about as well as Feisty, although it saw the
BIOS Raid array. It just failed trying to write to it. OpenSUSE did the
same.

So then I went back into the BIOS and deleted the Raid 1 array. Then I
started trying the install process again, this time intending to use
Linux' software Raid 1. Ubuntu (all three) failed again. Mandriva also
failed. OpenSUSE 10.2 almost worked. Its parition utility had the
capability of creating software Raid 1 arrays, and I succeeded in doing
so for a 2 GB swap and a 45 GB ext3 main partition. (I left the rest of
the drives for later.) OpenSUSE got all the way through the install
routine (20 minutes from DVD version), but when it tried to reboot it
couldn;t find the kernel.

I'm especially dismayed at my beloved Ubuntu. Oh well. Tomorrow I'll
fiddle around some more and find something that works.

John -

Most likely you could achieve the results you want with Linux's
built-in software raid.  Many distros are starting to support
installing to software raid partitions now.  Software raid does not
require support from hardware.

Jason

Jason Martin wrote:
> Most likely you could achieve the results you want with Linux's
> built-in software raid.  Many distros are starting to support
> installing to software raid partitions now.  Software raid does not
> require support from hardware.
> 

I'll second this.  Beware of fakeraid.  Even if you get it
running, I can almost guarantee you will regret it later.  
Think about it.  Do you trust the BIOS/motherboard vendor
to be responsible for the integrity of your filesystem?
Linux software raid is likely to be faster as well as more
reliable.  The only downside I can see is that it might create
some issues were you to want to create a dual boot system.

galen

> galen

With software RAID, you mirror partitions, not entire disks.  This
means you can leave a partition unRAIDed for that second OS.  Though a
good, spendy RAID controller card may allow for partition RAIDing at
the hardware level, you won't find this feature with the El Crappo
onboard RAID we're discussing here.

One bummer about software RAID is that you'll have to monkey around a
bit to mount the metadisks under Knoppix.  But it's not too big a
deal.  Same problem with LVM: http://www.knoppix.net/wiki/LVM2.

Carlos Konstanski

Umm. I don't put servers into production at work without at least 
RAID-1 protection. I know from first-hand experience that Debian (both 
sarge and etch) and CentOS (and ergo RHEL) can do software RAID with 
little difficulty.

Perhaps it's not obvious how to do it -- especially if you're using an 
idiosyncratic partitioning scheme (as I often do) -- but it's not 
extremely hard to do on Debian and CentOS. I'd be astonished if 
related distros like Ubuntu and Fedora didn't offer the same facility.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com


From creswick at gmail.com  Fri Mar 23 09:40:46 2007
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Fri, 23 Mar 2007 09:40:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] BIOS, Raid 1 and Confusion
In-Reply-To: <20070323092646.532ed55f.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070322194332.d79c2f4e.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<2e42bd9b0703222114k5dff6a53k526849979a99ed79@mail.gmail.com>
	<20070322235757.1a4fce3b.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<c29537840703230746y712653d2gf62251429b8fb142@mail.gmail.com>
	<4603F1F6.5070502@seitzassoc.com>
	<20070323092646.532ed55f.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <c834ec80703230940j47bb1008ha9955e87ab354b73@mail.gmail.com>

On 3/23/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> Raids is OpenSUSE 10.2. And, while it created the partitions and
> installed itself without issue, it won't boot.

I used to run a software raid (a striping raid -- raid-0?) and when
setting it up, I found that the easiest (by far) way to do it was to
put the kernel & boot "stuff" on a non-raided partition, so the OS
could actually get going and load the software raid config from /etc
after the actual OS core was in memory.  You can do it with the whole
OS on raided partitions, but you may need to monkey around with
initrd's and/or custom kernels to do so.  (I'd imagine this will
depend greatly on what kernels are available for the distros you're
using.)

Based on your problems so far, this could be what you're running into.
 On the other hand, I haven't toyed with Raid in ~2 years, so things
may have improved, and new problems may exist.

--Rogan
