> >> faster as well as more reliable.  The only downside I can see is
> >> that it might create some issues were you to want to create a dual
> >> boot system.

>
> Umm. I don't put servers into production at work without at least
> RAID-1 protection. I know from first-hand experience that Debian (both
> sarge and etch) and CentOS (and ergo RHEL) can do software RAID with
> little difficulty.
>
> Perhaps it's not obvious how to do it -- especially if you're using an
> idiosyncratic partitioning scheme (as I often do) -- but it's not
> extremely hard to do on Debian and CentOS. I'd be astonished if
> related distros like Ubuntu and Fedora didn't offer the same facility.

Dunno about Ubuntu, but Fedora definitely supports software raid in
the installer.  Additionally you can setup LVM on top of the software
raid from within the installer, in case you plan to grow any
partitions in the future.

> On Fri, 23 Mar 2007 08:27:50 -0700
> Galen Seitz <galens at seitzassoc.com> dijo:
>
>> Jason Martin wrote:
>> > Most likely you could achieve the results you want with Linux's
>> > built-in software raid.  Many distros are starting to support
>> > installing to software raid partitions now.  Software raid does not
>> > require support from hardware.
>> >
>>
>> I'll second this.  Beware of fakeraid.  Even if you get it
>> running, I can almost guarantee you will regret it later.
>> Think about it.  Do you trust the BIOS/motherboard vendor
>> to be responsible for the integrity of your filesystem?
>> Linux software raid is likely to be faster as well as more
>> reliable.  The only downside I can see is that it might create
>> some issues were you to want to create a dual boot system.
>
> I'm definitely going to take this advice. However, so far the only
> distro whose partition utility seems to be able to create software
> Raids is OpenSUSE 10.2. And, while it created the partitions and
> installed itself without issue, it won't boot. Maybe the others can
> create sofware Raids, but I just didn't click on the right buttons.
> I'll fiddle with them some more. Meantime, I deleted the Raid setup
> that I created in the BIOS previously.
>
> I also need to read up on what to do in case of a disk failure. The
> whole point of Raid 1 is redundancy for disasters. But I've never used
> Raid 1 before, nor have I experienced such a disaster, so it sounds
> like a good idea to figure out in advance what the recovery procedures
> are.

The claim that that suse is the only installer that can do RAID is simply
not true.  I have personally used redhat's and centos' Disk Druid to do
RAID, and I know that it can be done on fedora too, since I watched
someone do it.  I have also used suse's installer to create LVM, and found
it to be a bit more intuitive to use, so I can understand that you are
more easily able to figure out how to do it in suse than in some other
disk wizard (like Disk Druid).  If I did it more than once a year, and I
had the clicks committed to memory, I'd give instructions for redhat here.
 But alas, I use gentoo exclusively these days, where everything is done
manually.

Maybe you should boot up under knoppix, and play around with building and
breaking mirrors, before putting your root filesystem on one.  As long as
your mirrors are properly built, the procedure for swapping out a failed
disk is simple (raidhotadd).

http://tldp.org/HOWTO/Software-RAID-HOWTO.html is a complete RAID
tutorial.  It discusses how to simulate a drive failure for testing
purposes, among other topics.

Carlos Konstanski

From dmandel at pdxLinux.org  Fri Mar 23 12:12:50 2007
From: dmandel at pdxLinux.org (David Mandel)
Date: Fri, 23 Mar 2007 11:12:50 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] When to create private/public ssh keys
Message-ID: <eac729460703231212kaf6afa4ja855fec141b844d7@mail.gmail.com>

I'm a bit curious about how people handle their ssh keys as I'm never
sure when I should generate new keys and when I should make copies of
existing keys.

I always use ssh-keygen to create a new private/public key pair
anytime I create a new user - even if I'm the only one who will ever
be allowed to login as that user.  (I only do this when I want to new
user to use ssh.)

I also create a new private/public key pair anytime I add one of my
users to a new computer; so I end up having a different private/public
key pair for every user on every computer they exist on.

This seems like a fairly secure way of doing things, but leads to
maintenance headaches, in that I need to update my authorized_keys
files a lot.  I'm always tempted to copy a user's private/public key
pair from one computer to another when I add an existing user to a new
computer, but then I don't do it as that assumes I have good security
on all these machines.  But of course, some of them are laptops which
are subject to thief and what not.

Is these a better, easier way of doing things?

Sorry for the late reply.  It appears that the ati driver is a wrapper

something else for another.  I didn't find this information right off.

http://www.die.net/doc/linux/man/man4/radeon.4.html

problem must have been the ati driver not properly discerning the laptop 
display vs the external port.  I was getting impatient and trying many 
things in the end.

I have a mythtv box that I recently set up and it's been working fine
except I've had two instances where the time suddenly drifts way out of
wack.

Yesterday it seemed fine, but suddenly at about 5:00pm the time loses 23
minutes.  If I check the hardware clock as well it has dropped 23
minutes as well.  I've never seen anything like it.  Below is log
entries from syslog for ntp for the server....  So if I'm reading this
right at 16:48 everything is fine and 17 minutes later it's off by 1415
seconds?

Two questions

1) What could cause this and how do I fix it?
2) Is there a way to detect this and write a script to run ntpdate,
hwclock and restart ntp?

It's really annoying because 1/2 my show get's cut off.....

Sean

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Mar 23 15:20:59 2007
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 23 Mar 2007 15:20:59 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] ntp weirdness
In-Reply-To: <46044CA8.2010702@gmail.com>
References: <46044CA8.2010702@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.0.83.0703231457090.20903@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Fri, 23 Mar 2007, Sean Whitney wrote:

> I have a mythtv box that I recently set up and it's been working 
> fine except I've had two instances where the time suddenly drifts 
> way out of wack.
>
> Yesterday it seemed fine, but suddenly at about 5:00pm the time 
> loses 23 minutes.  If I check the hardware clock as well it has 
> dropped 23 minutes as well.  I've never seen anything like it. 
> Below is log entries from syslog for ntp for the server....  So if 
> I'm reading this right at 16:48 everything is fine and 17 minutes 
> later it's off by 1415 seconds?
>
> Two questions
>
> 1) What could cause this and how do I fix it?
> 2) Is there a way to detect this and write a script to run ntpdate,
> hwclock and restart ntp?

Back-at-you questions:

  1. Do you have ntp configured to write a drift file?

     Unless your box' main clock is seriously out of whack, a working
     drift file should keep the clock close to good in case...

  2. Did your mythtv box lose network connectivity for a long time?

     Long network outages will confuse hosts with wacky clocks.

  3. Do you have ntp configured to poll multiple servers?

     If your machine is only polling one server, then things can go
     bad quickly. Three "server" lines are good, e.g.,

     server 0.us.pool.ntp.org
     server 1.us.pool.ntp.org
     server 2.us.pool.ntp.org

If those aren't your issues, then you can try

  1. Forgoing ntpd, using ntpdate every X minutes from cron.

     This is a terrible solution, but unless your local clock is
     simply hideous, it'll keep you close.

  2. Keeping track of your ntpd's stratum and restarting ntpd whenever
     it rises above, say, 5. Run a cron job something like

     STRATUM=$(ntpq -c rl | grep -o "stratum=[0-9]*" | cut -d= -f2)
     test $STRATUM -gt 5 && /etc/init.d/ntpd restart

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From sean.whitney at gmail.com  Fri Mar 23 16:24:29 2007
From: sean.whitney at gmail.com (Sean Whitney)

Subject: [PLUG] ntp weirdness
In-Reply-To: <46045E17.7010805@gmail.com>

	<46045E17.7010805@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <460461AD.1010108@gmail.com>

Well I added this to /etc/cron.hourly

#!/bin/sh

STRATUM=$(/usr/bin/ntpq -c rl | grep -o "stratum=[0-9]*" | cut -d= -f2)

if [ $STRATUM -gt 5 ] || [ -z $STRATUM ]; then
        /usr/sbin/ntpdate-debian
        /sbin/hwclock --systohc
        /etc/init.d/ntp restart
        /usr/bin/logger Reset Time
fi

We'll see if this ever kicks in.....


Sean

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Mar 23 16:24:15 2007
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 23 Mar 2007 16:24:15 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] ntp weirdness

	<alpine.OSX.0.83.0703231457090.20903@arrowhead.galois.com>
	<46045E17.7010805@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.0.83.0703231611400.20903@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Fri, 23 Mar 2007, Sean Whitney wrote:

>>  2. Keeping track of your ntpd's stratum and restarting ntpd
>>     whenever it rises above, say, 5. Run a cron job something like
>>
>>     STRATUM=$(ntpq -c rl | grep -o "stratum=[0-9]*" | cut -d= -f2)
>>     test $STRATUM -gt 5 && /etc/init.d/ntpd restart
>
> Thanks, I'll give this a shot...

The caveat here is that the stratum won't typically rise to its 
expected level for several minutes after a restart. If you want to 
run this test every couple of minutes, it's probably a good idea to 
make sure ntpd isn't in startup mode, e.g.,

   # bail if /var/run/ntpd.pid isn't at least 10 minutes old
   PIDFILE=$(find /var/run/ntpd.pid -mmin -10)
   test -n "$PIDFILE" && exit

or something like that...

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> dijo:

> Now I'm going to go beat up Debian some more to see if I can figure out

Well that didn't go well.

I finally figured out how to create the RAID partitions with the Debian
amd64 installer. That user interface needs serious work. Like scap it
and start over from the beginning. :(

I created a 2 GB swap on sda1 and a 45 GB ext3 partition for / on sda2,
mirrored to their counterparts: sdb1 and sdb2. After I finally
successfully created them the install utility proceeded to install the
rest of Debian Etch -- three hours downloading. When it finished it
prompted to restart the computer. When the computer restarted I got a
lot of stuff, ending in:

RPL-ROM-ADR: 0050 8DCA 55FF
RPL-ROM-IRQ: 10
RPL-ROM-PIO: CC00
RPL-ROM-FFC: 1 (which added 1 to the number every second or so until it
hit 50, then the computer hung.)

The above appeared before Grub or anything related to Linux started to
appear. I don't know where to look to find out what it means. But my
guess is that it means something couldn't find an OS to boot to.

So then I tried the Ubuntu Feisty amd64 beta that I downloaded this
morning. What I didn't realize as I was downloading it was that the
Alternate CD is not a live/install CD -- it is install only. OK, fine.
Debian Etch is hosed anyway, so I decided to go ahead and install
Feisty. But that went no better. Worse, in fact. I couldn't get past
the partitioning part. It refused to delete the Debian RAID partitions
and it refused to use them. There was an option to reformat them, but
it errored out.

For my next exercise I used a Knoppix live CD, intending to see if I
could find /boot/grub/menu.lst. And it turned out to be right where it
is supposed to be. That is, on the Knoppix desktop there are four items
(the two hard disks are matching Western Digital 320 GB SATAII disks):
two icons for WDC3200 which are sda1 and sdb1 (the swap), and two icons
labled WDC3200 for sda2 and sdb2 (the root partition). And menu.lst is
right there on sda2 and sdb2 in /boot/grub. The menu items are:

title	Debian GNU/Linux, kernel 2.6.18-4-amd64
root	(hd0,1)
kernel:	/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.10-4-amd64 root=/dev/md1 ro
initrd	/boot/initrd.img-2.6.18-4-amd64
savedefault

The second one is identical, but it says (single user mode) at the end
of the title line.

I note that it is pointing to /dev/md1, and I recall during the setup
that md1 is what the partition utility called the RAID device. As far
as I can tell the menu.lst entries are correct.

While writing this it occurred to me that the BIOS is not finding the
mbr. But I am not sure what an mbr does, let alone how to fix it. Or if
my supposition is even correct.

I should apply for university credit for all I am learning. :) 

Before I wipe out everything and start over yet again, suggestions
would be welcome.

> I finally figured out how to create the RAID partitions with the Debian

>
> I created a 2 GB swap on sda1 and a 45 GB ext3 partition for / on sda2,
> mirrored to their counterparts: sdb1 and sdb2. After I finally
> successfully created them the install utility proceeded to install the
> rest of Debian Etch -- three hours downloading. When it finished it
> prompted to restart the computer. When the computer restarted I got a
> lot of stuff, ending in:
>
> RPL-ROM-ADR: 0050 8DCA 55FF
> RPL-ROM-IRQ: 10
> RPL-ROM-PIO: CC00
> RPL-ROM-FFC: 1 (which added 1 to the number every second or so until it
> hit 50, then the computer hung.)


Thats your machine trying to PXE boot. Its looking for a DHCP address from a
networked machine to catch and install image from. You can change the PXE in
your BIOS so it doesn't do that anymore. I've setup RAID on Redhat box years
ago but it was with a Highpoint raid card. You may have mentioned this and I
missed it, but what kind of onboard RAID do you have? Both Promise and High
Point provide Linux drivers, that atleast with Redhat/Fedora you can preload
before the installer starts so that you can setup those RAID partitions
properly. It took me and my co-worker days to get that fscking stuff working
so don't lose hope and keep us updated on your progress.

FWIW There's an old urban legend in the Slackware world that you _must_
drink a beer while installing or something will go wrong. Maybe its time to
crack a beer?

Drew-

other stuff to tweak. And I also found an option to disable RAID on the
motherboard. After I rebooted, Grub came up and Debian launched!

Problem: While the above is significant progress (first time I've
gotten an OS on this computer to actually boot), there is a problem
with USB. The keyboard and mouse are both USB. I can log in, and after
logging in I get popup help balloons when I mouse over them. But the
second I click on something no more popup balloons and the keyboard is
dead. Strangely, I can move the mouse cursor around, although I can no
longer click on anything.

Back to the BIOS. I bet one of the tweaks I did goobered up some USB
thing in there.

> FWIW There's an old urban legend in the Slackware world that you _must_
> drink a beer while installing or something will go wrong. Maybe its time to
> crack a beer?

I'm on my second. :)

Thanks for the insight!

> Well that didn't go well.
>
> I finally figured out how to create the RAID partitions with the Debian
> amd64 installer. That user interface needs serious work. Like scap it
> and start over from the beginning. :(
>
> I created a 2 GB swap on sda1 and a 45 GB ext3 partition for / on sda2,
> mirrored to their counterparts: sdb1 and sdb2. After I finally

By the way, software raid on swap partitions is rather pointless, and
may in fact make swap slower.  You really should just create two
normal swap partitions, one on each drive, but not raid.  The kernel
will use them in a nice efficient manner to maximize performance.

Jason

I went back into the BIOS and noted that framebuffer was set to 64 MB.
I increased it to 128 MB and then the mouse worked for about 15 minutes
before dying. Strange that the cursor still works, though. I remember
reading in the moherboard manual that the nVidia GeForce could use up
to 256 MB, so I went back into the BIOS and raised it to 256 MB.
Afterwards it was worse than with 128 MB. 

I also noted during the 15 minutes when I had the mouse working that
the display was set to 1024 x 768 and there were no higher options. I
think Debian Etch is using Vesa drivers instead of nVidia drivers (nv
driver). 

It's bedtime and I give up. Tomorrow I think I'm going to install FC 6
amd64, that I finished downloading tonight. F7 test2 was the best at
detecting the video and even the monitor. Maybe FC 6 will do so as
well. And after that I'll try the latest Feisty amd64.

Making progress. Learning. Thanks for the help.

Fedora Core 6 amd64 is installed, replacing Debian Etch. There is good
news and there is bad news.

First, I took Jason's suggestion that the swap didn't need to be on a
RAID, so I deleted that RAID in the FC6 partition editor, then told it
to reformat the ext3 RAID partition for /. FC6 has a much better
partition editor -- a GUI and even the mouse works in it. But I am
assuming that now that I have two separate swap partitions, each of 2
GB, that Linux will be using them both, so I really have 4 GB of swap?
If so, I should probably cut them back to 1 GB each. The computer has 2
GB of RAM, so 4 GB of swap is probably unnecessary.

The good news is that FC6 autodetected the nVidia GeForce 6100 and also
autodetected the Viewsonic Graphics Series G90f, listing them both by
name in the configuration tool. It set up the resolution at 1600 x 1200
too. This is exactly what the Fedora 7 test2 live/install CD did.
Nothing else I have tried has detected either one -- all they gave me
was Vesa.

There are three bad newses. First, the ethernet on the motherboard
(gigabit) was not detected. Debian Etch had no problem finding it and
setting it up, nor did the F7 test2 live/install CD, nor did the
Knoppix live CD have any problem with it. It's a Realtek, and so is the
sound. And the second bad news is that the sound doesn't work either,
although the sound also worked fine with the live CDs and with Debian
Etch. The ethernet and the sound must be related. The ethernet light on
the connector is lit up and so is the light on the switch, so the
connection is still good. And for even more strangitude, look at this
that I copied over from a terminal window:

jjj at localhost -j$ su
Password:
root at localhost jjj|# lspci
bash: lspci: command not found
root at localhost jjj|# lsmod
bash: lsmodi: command not found
root at localhost jjj|# ifconfig
bash: ifconfig: command not found

WTF?

However, dmesg works. Besides the same lines about the mouse and USB
that I posted earlier, I found:

NET: Registered protocol family #

This line appeared several times, each time with a different number for
#. And that is the only line in the entire dmesg output that had anything to do with networking.

The third bad news is that the mouse problem continues. The cursor
continues to work, but the click function dies anywhere from right
after the first click up to maybe 15 minutes. The keyboard still works.
If I Ctrl-Alt-Bkspc to log out and back in again, the mouse is usually
restored but, again, just for a short while. I did determine that it is
not the USB, because I plugged in an old wired Logitech and it died the
same as the new one. 

First step is to get the ethernet working. Can't fix much without a net
connection. So now I'm off to google and check out FC to see if they
have forums and wikis and stuff.

Fedora Core 6 amd64 is installed, replacing Debian Etch. There is good
news and there is bad news.

First, I took Jason's suggestion that the swap didn't need to be on a
RAID, so I deleted that RAID in the FC6 partition editor, then told it
to reformat the ext3 RAID partition for /. FC6 has a much better
partition editor -- a GUI and even the mouse works in it. But I am
assuming that now that I have two separate swap partitions, each of 2
GB, that Linux will be using them both, so I really have 4 GB of swap?
If so, I should probably cut them back to 1 GB each. The computer has 2
GB of RAM, so 4 GB of swap is probably unnecessary.

The good news is that FC6 autodetected the nVidia GeForce 6100 and also
autodetected the Viewsonic Graphics Series G90f, listing them both by
name in the configuration tool. It set up the resolution at 1600 x 1200
too. This is exactly what the Fedora 7 test2 live/install CD did.
Nothing else I have tried has detected either one -- all they gave me
was Vesa.

There are three bad newses. First, the ethernet on the motherboard
(gigabit) was not detected. Debian Etch had no problem finding it and
setting it up, nor did the F7 test2 live/install CD, nor did the
Knoppix live CD have any problem with it. It's a Realtek, and so is the
sound. And the second bad news is that the sound doesn't work either,
although the sound also worked fine with the live CDs and with Debian
Etch. The ethernet and the sound must be related. The ethernet light on
the connector is lit up and so is the light on the switch, so the
connection is still good. And for even more strangitude, look at this
that I copied over from a terminal window:

jjj at localhost -j$ su
Password:
root at localhost jjj|# lspci
bash: lspci: command not found
root at localhost jjj|# lsmod
bash: lsmodi: command not found
root at localhost jjj|# ifconfig
bash: ifconfig: command not found

WTF?

However, dmesg works. Besides the same lines about the mouse and USB
that I posted earlier, I found:

NET: Registered protocol family #

This line appeared several times, each time with a different number for
#. And that is the only line in the entire dmesg output that had anything to
do with networking.

The third bad news is that the mouse problem continues. The cursor
continues to work, but the click function dies anywhere from right
after the first click up to maybe 15 minutes. The keyboard still works.
If I Ctrl-Alt-Bkspc to log out and back in again, the mouse is usually
restored but, again, just for a short while. I did determine that it is
not the USB, because I plugged in an old wired Logitech and it died the
same as the new one. 

First step is to get the ethernet working. Can't fix much without a net
connection. So now I'm off to google and check out FC to see if they
have forums and wikis and stuff.

Subject: [PLUG] Linux USB Hardware Prototyping

Hardware geeks only ...

Anybody here do USB prototyping.

I want to build an interface board that goes from a chip test
socket to high-speed USB.  An easy-to-use Linux-friendly digital
I/O board with about 12 digital gozoutas (2 fast) to the interface
board, and 8 digital gozintas, would be nice.  I want 10MBytes per
second read rates, faster if possible.  FIFOs and agile clock
generation a plus.

I will not be building a product, so cost is not as important as
ease-of-use.  I will be building very long stimulus patterns out of
nested software loops, and capturing the results.   Hopefully the
programming interface is simple and flexible enough to facilitate this.

Two possibilities found on the web, there are probably more:

Devasys USB I2C/IO based on the Cypress AN2131QC http://snurl.com/mf21
         ( http://www.devasys.com/usbi2cio.htm )
Celeritous USBDEV1 based on the ftdi  FT2232C   http://snurl.com/mf1y

Suggestions? 

Keith

From ehem at m5p.com  Sat Mar 24 11:16:09 2007
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sat, 24 Mar 2007 11:16:09 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Debian install and RAID 1
In-Reply-To: <20070324175320.E008142A0@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200703241816.l2OIG9s9070066@m5p.com>

>From: "Jason Martin" <nsxfreddy at gmail.com>
> By the way, software raid on swap partitions is rather pointless, and
> may in fact make swap slower.  You really should just create two
> normal swap partitions, one on each drive, but not raid.  The kernel
> will use them in a nice efficient manner to maximize performance.

Stripping swap slices with Linux is pointless, as doing multiple swap
slices is faster. Mirroring or any of the other true RAID modes isn't
pointless with swap. Multiple swap slices won't give you the redundancy,
merely be simpler than stripes. Though if you lose a disk you may have to
restart anyway.

I have used the FDTI chips for one project but it was under Windows.  They have
supported the people doing serial drivers for the chip well enough
that these are
now in the kernel.  The ftdi2232C is a dual uart part that can also be
used for bit banging
12 IO lines per side.  Prototype boards are available direct from
http://www.dlpdesign.com/
Mouser and Digikey.

My project used the IO,Serial, and I2C off of the chip.  Most time
critical stuff was done
in hardware at the other end of the I2C but I did do some 400khz clock
trains for debugging
the hardware.


From m0gely at telestream.com  Sat Mar 24 11:25:32 2007
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Sat, 24 Mar 2007 11:25:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Freegeek computers and support?
In-Reply-To: <45FEEE36.3000905@gmail.com>
References: <45FC054C.7040205@gmail.com>	<20070319192048.GA19186@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<45FEEE36.3000905@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <46056D1C.5030405@telestream.com>

Robert Citek wrote:

> I'm not sure what that means.  I understand that if I buy a StoreBox it
> comes without support from FreeGeek.  Fair enough.  But let's say I want
> to connect my StoreBox to the internet via DSL.  Although I could
> probably do it myself, let's say I'd rather just pay someone to do it
> (e.g. like Geek Squad.)  Is there anyone I can call to do it for me?  If
> so, who?

The problem is there is too many distro's and GUI's out there with their 
own home baked methodologies.  Now, for DSL, or cable it's much less of 
an issue as any modern OS will plugin into a network/DSL/cable and work 
by default using DHCP.  But for slightly more specialized configurations 
it can all change.  GUI tools will be different each direction you look. 
  Command line methods will all largely be the same, but that can be a 
task in an of itself.  One time I spent 5 minutes on the phone just 
trying to get a guy to press the Start button, then Run, they type in 
the letters CMD and press OK.  I almost killed a very cute puppy!

This is all moot if you can get someone to just come out and get you set 
up.  But if your distro has a firewall enabled by default, then the 
support person may need to know that now too.  You then get into a level 
of knowledge where people don't want to be bothered helping (read: drive 
out) others with such remedial tasks.  I could go on, but the point here 
is it will be much easier to find help where it already exists in the 
Linux world.  Such as this mailing list, or in the case of buying a 
computer from Freegeek, knowing about opportunities there such as a 
clinic, or helping behind the scenes with some donated labor and 
learning that way.

Geeksquad.... *shudder*

-- 
- m0gely

On 3/24/07, Elliott Mitchell <ehem at m5p.com> wrote:
> >From: "Jason Martin" <nsxfreddy at gmail.com>
> > By the way, software raid on swap partitions is rather pointless, and
> > may in fact make swap slower.  You really should just create two
> > normal swap partitions, one on each drive, but not raid.  The kernel
> > will use them in a nice efficient manner to maximize performance.
>
> Stripping swap slices with Linux is pointless, as doing multiple swap
> slices is faster. Mirroring or any of the other true RAID modes isn't
> pointless with swap. Multiple swap slices won't give you the redundancy,
> merely be simpler than stripes. Though if you lose a disk you may have to
> restart anyway.

Unless your desktop motherboard supports hot-swap hard drives, I doubt
you're going to survive a disk crash without a hard reboot.  In this
case, mirroring your swap is just eating extra disk space.

Jason

I think I found the solution for the ethernet on the Fedora Hardware forum. This person has the same motherboard and started a thread about ethernet not working. At the end of the thread he said:

---------------------
I downloaded from http://download.fedora.redhat.com/p...updates/6/i386/
the kernel-xen-2.6.19-1.2911.fc6.i686.rpm, mkinitrd-5.1.19.0.3-1.i386.rpm, nash-5.1.19.0.3-1.i386.rpm, parted-1.8.2-1.fc6.i386.rpm. I burned them on a disc, and a rpm -Uvh solved the problem.
---------------------

So I downloaded the files, burned them to a CD, and got them into the Desktop on the new computer. But I'm just a dumbass Debian dude; RPMs are a mystery to me. I read man rpm and then tried to install them, but got:

root at localhost Desktop]# rpm -Uvh *
warning mkinitrd-5.1.19.0.3-1.i386.rpm Header V3 DSA signature: NOKEY, key ID 4f2ab6fd2
Preparing ... ###### [100%]
file /usr/share/info/parted.info.gz from install of parted-1.8.2-1.fc6 conflicts with file from package parted-1.8.1-1.fc6
file /usr/share/iman/man8/parted.8.gz from install of parted-1.8.2-1.fc6 conflicts with file from package parted-1.8.1-1.fc6
file /sbin/mkinitrd from install of mkinitrd-5.1.19.0.3-1 conflicts with file from package mkinitrd-5.1.19.0.2-1

I tried removing the old packages first, but couldn't the syntax right. The man page also talked about installing the newer package and removing the old one in the same command, but I couldn't get that right either. 

I'm sure the whole problem with ethernet and sound is that the live CDs and the Debian Etch that I installed all used later drivers and kernels than FC6. These updates should do the trick, if I can just figure out how to install them from the command line.

rpm -e --nodeps <package name>
rpm -i --nodeps <package name>


From ehem at m5p.com  Sat Mar 24 13:23:45 2007
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sat, 24 Mar 2007 13:23:45 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Debian install and RAID 1
In-Reply-To: <20070324185641.481204440@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200703242023.l2OKNjGC070631@m5p.com>

>From: "Jason Martin" <nsxfreddy at gmail.com>
> On 3/24/07, Elliott Mitchell <ehem at m5p.com> wrote:
> > >From: "Jason Martin" <nsxfreddy at gmail.com>
> > > By the way, software raid on swap partitions is rather pointless, and
> > > may in fact make swap slower.  You really should just create two
> > > normal swap partitions, one on each drive, but not raid.  The kernel
> > > will use them in a nice efficient manner to maximize performance.
> >
> > Stripping swap slices with Linux is pointless, as doing multiple swap
> > slices is faster. Mirroring or any of the other true RAID modes isn't
> > pointless with swap. Multiple swap slices won't give you the redundancy,
> > merely be simpler than stripes. Though if you lose a disk you may have to
> > restart anyway.
> 
> Unless your desktop motherboard supports hot-swap hard drives, I doubt
> you're going to survive a disk crash without a hard reboot.  In this
> case, mirroring your swap is just eating extra disk space.

Does allow you to decide /when/ the downtime will be, potentially very
important. Also gives you a hint of what is up before you get random
crashes.

> Preparing ... ###### [100%]
> file /usr/share/info/parted.info.gz from install of parted-1.8.2-1.fc6 conflicts with file from package parted-1.8.1-1.fc6
> file /usr/share/iman/man8/parted.8.gz from install of parted-1.8.2-1.fc6 conflicts with file from package parted-1.8.1-1.fc6
> file /sbin/mkinitrd from install of mkinitrd-5.1.19.0.3-1 conflicts with file from package mkinitrd-5.1.19.0.2-1
>
> I tried removing the old packages first, but couldn't the syntax right. The man page also talked about installing the newer package and removing the old one in the same command, but I couldn't get that right either.
>
> I'm sure the whole problem with ethernet and sound is that the live CDs and the Debian Etch that I installed all used later drivers and kernels than FC6. These updates should do the trick, if I can just figure out how to install them from the command line.

You said above that you installed the amd64 version of FC6, but here
you downloaded the i686 version of the kernel.  You need to download
the kernel with x86_64 extension.

Most likely the kernel that comes with the FC6 install does not have
the driver support for your NIC, so you just need to upgrade to the
latest kernel *that matches your architecture*.

Beware of FC7-test2, as that is just starting to stabilize and
upgrades are not officially supported in FC test images.  I'd stick
with FC6 unless you're trying to be more bleeding edge and you don't
mind reinstalling in May (and possibly two other times between now and
then for test3 and test4).

Jason

I followed the instructions I found on a Fedora forum. The person who
posted the instructions had the same exact motherboard and said the
instructions solved the problem. The kernel I downloaded was exactly
what he listed in his instructions. I'm not doubting you. Just
explaining why I did what I did. But the issue is now moot because I
have nuked the FC6 installation and installed Feisty instead. However,
Feisty has its issues too -- the RAID partition utility in the
Alternate CD is broken. I had to install it on a single partition on
one of the drives. So my next foray into distro-swap world will be F7
test2 amd64. The download will finish in about 20 minutes, then I'll
take it for a spin.

> Most likely the kernel that comes with the FC6 install does not have
> the driver support for your NIC, so you just need to upgrade to the
> latest kernel *that matches your architecture*.

I am positive you are correct. The evidence is overwhelming. Everything
that has a recent kernel has no problem with the network and sound.
Kernel 2.6.18 and earlier do. For example, F7 test2 i386 had no
problem, nor did Knoppix 5.1 or Feisty amd64. But Dapper, Edgy, FC6 and
Knoppix 5.0 did. From that guy's instructions I'm pretty sure it takes
2.6.19 or later.

> Beware of FC7-test2, as that is just starting to stabilize and
> upgrades are not officially supported in FC test images.  I'd stick
> with FC6 unless you're trying to be more bleeding edge and you don't
> mind reinstalling in May (and possibly two other times between now and
> then for test3 and test4).

It won't be a problem installing a new OS every now and then on this
machine. Its purpose is to give me networked space for backups from my
laptop and from my Windows 2000 desktop, and to function as a failsafe
computer to get on the net with when I mess up my laptop again. I want
the two 320 GB SATA II drives with a small swap, a RAID 1 partition of
about 45 GB for the OS, and the remainder (275 GB) as a RAID 1 place
for the backups. So if I have to wipe out the OS on the second
partitiion from time to time, it won't be a problem. Therefore I don't
mind being a little bit bleeding edge. 

I know I will get the network and sound problem fixed. The mouse
problem is more vexing. The mouse cursor continues to work, but the
click function dies anywhere from right after the first click to maybe
15 minutes after booting. Ctrl-Alt-Bkspc and logging back in again
restores it. The mouse is part of a Logitech combo USB keyboard/mouse
deal. But it's not a USB problem because I tried a ten-year old wired
mouse and the same thing happened. And it has happened reliably with
every single OS I have tried, regardless of kernel.

I've mucked around in the BIOS until there's nothing left to try. Right
now I suspect an IRQ conflict and that's what I'm going to fiddle with
next.

I so love breaking brand new computers. :)

On 3/24/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> On Sat, 24 Mar 2007 20:50:16 -0700
> "drew wymore" <drew.wymore at gmail.com> dijo:
>
> > > I know I will get the network and sound problem fixed. The mouse
> > > problem is more vexing. The mouse cursor continues to work, but the
> > > click function dies anywhere from right after the first click to maybe
> > > 15 minutes after booting. Ctrl-Alt-Bkspc and logging back in again
> > > restores it. The mouse is part of a Logitech combo USB keyboard/mouse
> > > deal. But it's not a USB problem because I tried a ten-year old wired
> > > mouse and the same thing happened. And it has happened reliably with
> > > every single OS I have tried, regardless of kernel.
> > >
> > > I've mucked around in the BIOS until there's nothing left to try. Right
> > > now I suspect an IRQ conflict and that's what I'm going to fiddle with
> > > next.
>
> > There's a BIOS option that lets the OS manage IRQ's (windows) or the BIOS I
> > can't recall what its called but you want to change it to NO so that the
> > IRQ's are handled internally by the MOBO and not the OS. Have you mucked
> > around with the X config at all?
>
> No, and about half an hour ago I decided that it might be in X instead
> of an IRQ issue. I was installing F7 test2 and the install utility
> (Anaconda) is a GUI with the mouse active. I did not have one single
> problem with the mouse, even though I was clicking all over the place.
> But the GUI in Anaconda is different from what you get when you finally
> boot the finished OS. If it's not X, then maybe X is the problem. Or
> maybe the problem is in Gnome.

Anaconda is just a bunch of Python sitting on top of X.  Perhaps you
can try to figure out what is different from the install CD X
environment and the installed environment.  X has its own log file,
usually in /var/log.  You can also compare xorg.conf files, which on
an installed system are in /etc/X11.  Not sure where the installer CD
has it, it may be dynamically generated.

> I'll check out the IRQ options in the BIOS.

It would be interesting to see your /proc/interrupts.

> Fedora 7 test2 amd64 did not go well. The first problem was Anaconda,
> which halted just after reading its initial starting screens from the
> CD drive. It insisted it didn't know what driver to use for the CD
> drive. Mind you it had been reading it for several minutes already. It
> asked me to select a driver from a list or insert a driver disk. I had
> no idea what to do. Finally, after retrying half a dozen times, I
> accidentally clicked on the first driver in the list, whereupon it
> happily finished the installation from the DVD. Lame, but it's still
> alpha.

There's actually a fairly easy explaination for the discrepancy
between reading the drive for several minutes and *then* not finding
the driver.  Linux distros usually use an initrd image, which is
loaded into RAM by the bootloader, along with the kernel image.  The
initrd contains a basic environment, including a shell to execute
scripts and any kernel modules necessary for basic devices like
hardware.  In the case of install CDs the image is often enough to get
the installer going, but then if no driver is found for the PATA or
SATA devices the installer won't know where to install to.

> So then I finished the installation. But when it rebooted it hung on
> the first GUI window when it got to Starting Networking. I rebooted,
> but it always hangs right there. The kernel is 2.6.20. I noted during
> the installation that it detected the network card and I thought it set
> it up. (Sigh)

Try editing the grub command line and removing the rhgb portion.  This
will let you see the straight console, instead of the graphical boot
console, and you will be able to see problems like a kernel oops or
panic.

> I had such high hopes for F7 test2. :(

Well, I did warn you :-)

Jason

This morning I did some more installing/uninstalling of different distros (all 64-bit). I can now say with some confidence that the mouse problem is either in Gnome or in the nv driver. I had Simply Mepis loaded, which uses KDE and the video was Vesa -- not a single mouse problem for over an hour of poking around. I removed it because its kernel was too old to handle the gigabit and sound, and I couldn't find any way to upgrade the kernel without a net connection. 

I followed that with OpenSUSE 10.2, but its kernel was also too old for the gigabit and sound (2.6.18-2). I replaced it with Mandriva-free-2007. MF2007 properly identified the network card as a Realtek RTL-8169SC Gigabit Ethernet (rev 10). But it couldn't get it working. So then I went back to Fedora 7. The installation didn't specifically say it found the Realtek, but the installation went smoothly. However, when I rebooted it hung on Starting Networking, the same as it did before. Mouse and keyboard dead. It didn't set up a failsafe mode in Grub, but I went in and edited out the rghb as you suggested. This got me to:

Code: 41 8b 5d 00 85 db 0f 88 dc 03 00 00 f7 c3 00 20 00 74 60
RIP [<ffffffff8816f3e5>]  :r8169:rt8169_rx_interrup+0x5d/0x50e
RSP <ffff810037c97e10>
CR2: 0000000000000000
Kernel panic - not syncing: Aiee, killing interrupt handler!

Now, there is one thing that hits me between the eyes in the above -- the "8169." The Realtek is an RTL-8169SC. So it looks like what appeared on the graphical screen as a hang is a kernel panic caused by using the wrong IRQ for the Realtek.

So for my next exercise I decided to edit Grub some more. I tried irqpoll, but it still hung -- in a different place, but hung nevertheless. In the process I googled around and discovered that Realtek has a driver to make the 8169SC work with amd64 kernels older than 2.6.19-2. I downloaded it and it has a Readme with instructions. So I think what I am going to do next is go back to FC6 and then, instead of the fix that I found on the Fedora forums, I'll try installing the new driver.

> 
> So for my next exercise I decided to edit Grub some more. I tried irqpoll, but it still hung -- in a different place, but hung nevertheless. In the process I googled around and discovered that Realtek has a driver to make the 8169SC work with amd64 kernels older than 2.6.19-2. I downloaded it and it has a Readme with instructions. So I think what I am going to do next is go back to FC6 and then, instead of the fix that I found on the Fedora forums, I'll try installing the new driver.

I changed my mind about the driver. Instead I went over to my Windows
2000 desktop, stole its NIC, and put it in the new computer. Fedora 7
found it and happily got me on the net. And as soon as it was on the
net it announced I had 267 updates available. I told it to go ahead.
Three hours later it was finished and I rebooted. And when it came up I
went into Network and noticed that it said Realtek 8139 -- not 3Com501,
which is the card I stole from the Windows desktop. I thought this was
very strange, so I shut down removed the 3Com card that I stole from
the Windows desktop and rebooted. When it came back up it was still
using the onboard Realtek 8139. Uname -a tells me I now have kernel
2.6.20-1. 

The really strange thing is that previously I had disabled the onboard
Realtek in the BIOS. I had to do so to get Fedora 7 to boot. And now it
is working fine even though (I just checked) it is still disabled in
the BIOS. Oh well. Linux programmers must be truly clever to figure out
how to use a device that is not supposed to exist. :)

I'm really pleased with Fedora 7. Totally stable. Just one small
problem -- the clock is doing extremely strange things. It's running at
about double time, is paying no attentiion to UDP, and is paying no
attention to manual settings. Except, that is, that I can set it to,
say, 10:30 pm and after I finish it will say something like 3:15 am.
I'm talking about the standard clock in the Gnome panel. And I can set
the screensaver to 30 minutes and it kicks in at 12 minutes one time,
then 17 minutes the next time, and other random times. I know a
professor at PSU whose concept of time is similar, but I didn't realize
he was in charge of UDP.

I've signed up for Fedora forums and for the Fedora e-list. I'm on my
way to becoming a Fedorist.

Much thanks for all the suggestions. :)

On 3/25/07, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> I changed my mind about the driver. Instead I went over to my Windows
> 2000 desktop, stole its NIC, and put it in the new computer. Fedora 7
> found it and happily got me on the net. And as soon as it was on the
> net it announced I had 267 updates available. I told it to go ahead.
> Three hours later it was finished and I rebooted. And when it came up I
> went into Network and noticed that it said Realtek 8139 -- not 3Com501,
> which is the card I stole from the Windows desktop. I thought this was
> very strange, so I shut down removed the 3Com card that I stole from
> the Windows desktop and rebooted. When it came back up it was still
> using the onboard Realtek 8139. Uname -a tells me I now have kernel
> 2.6.20-1.
>
> The really strange thing is that previously I had disabled the onboard
> Realtek in the BIOS. I had to do so to get Fedora 7 to boot. And now it
> is working fine even though (I just checked) it is still disabled in
> the BIOS. Oh well. Linux programmers must be truly clever to figure out
> how to use a device that is not supposed to exist. :)

Linux rarely relies on BIOS routines to avoid running ancient 16-bit
code.  Also, you may have simply disabled *booting* from the NIC, not
actually disabled the NIC itself.

> I'm really pleased with Fedora 7. Totally stable. Just one small
> problem -- the clock is doing extremely strange things. It's running at
> about double time, is paying no attentiion to UDP, and is paying no
> attention to manual settings. Except, that is, that I can set it to,
> say, 10:30 pm and after I finish it will say something like 3:15 am.
> I'm talking about the standard clock in the Gnome panel. And I can set
> the screensaver to 30 minutes and it kicks in at 12 minutes one time,
> then 17 minutes the next time, and other random times. I know a
> professor at PSU whose concept of time is similar, but I didn't realize
> he was in charge of UDP.

I assume you mean NTP, not UDP which is a network transport :-)

There's a couple boot options you can try:
- clock=tsc
- noapic acpi=off

Chances are you need to find a BIOS update.

> I've signed up for Fedora forums and for the Fedora e-list. I'm on my
> way to becoming a Fedorist.

Be sure to check out the unofficial Fedora FAQ at http://www.fedorafaq.org/.

Jason

On Mon, 26 Mar 2007 09:42:27 -0700
"Jason Martin" <nsxfreddy at gmail.com> dijo:

> > The really strange thing is that previously I had disabled the onboard
> > Realtek in the BIOS. I had to do so to get Fedora 7 to boot. And now it
> > is working fine even though (I just checked) it is still disabled in
> > the BIOS. Oh well. Linux programmers must be truly clever to figure out
> > how to use a device that is not supposed to exist. :)
> 
> Linux rarely relies on BIOS routines to avoid running ancient 16-bit
> code.  Also, you may have simply disabled *booting* from the NIC, not
> actually disabled the NIC itself.

There is more weirdicity going on with the onboard Realtek gigabit. As
it turns out, I was wrong -- I had enabled it again in the BIOS. But
Administration > Network showed two instances of it when I had the
3Com501 installed, both pointing at the Realtek, and the second one
ended in .bak. And I could connect the cable to the 3Com or to the
connector from the motherboard and get on the network either way. When
I thought the Realtek was working by itself because of the kernel
upgrade I removed the 3Com. It booted normally and I still had network.
Administration > Network still showed the two instances of the Realtek.
I deleted the .bak version. When I rebooted it hung with the kernel
panic as previously. I had to disable the Realtek in the BIOS again to
get it to boot. I later discovered that it will boot fine with the
Realtek enabled, but only if there is another network card installed.
And the second network card will always be listed in Administration >
Network as a Realtek 8169 regardless of what it really is. And it will
be listed twice, the second one with the extension .bak.

> > I'm really pleased with Fedora 7. Totally stable. Just one small
> > problem -- the clock is doing extremely strange things. It's running at
> > about double time, is paying no attentiion to UDP, and is paying no
> > attention to manual settings. Except, that is, that I can set it to,
> > say, 10:30 pm and after I finish it will say something like 3:15 am.

> I assume you mean NTP, not UDP which is a network transport :-)

Yes.

> There's a couple boot options you can try:
> - clock=tsc
> - noapic acpi=off

According to dmesg I already have tsc.

> Chances are you need to find a BIOS update.

Already checked that out -- the motherboard came with the latest,
version 1.1, November 8, 2006.

> > I've signed up for Fedora forums and for the Fedora e-list. I'm on my
> > way to becoming a Fedorist.

> Be sure to check out the unofficial Fedora FAQ at http://www.fedorafaq.org/.

Already been there, but I need a lot more education on Fedora!

Right now I'm trying to figure out Yum, Yumex and Kyum. They are
pathetic. I seriously miss Synaptic! I found that you can install
Synaptic, and I did, but when I tried to launch it all I get is a popup
that says "Error!" That's all it says. I'd stick with Kyum if I could
at least figure out how to enable more repositories. The "Available"
button lists only 434 packages. I've poked at it for half an hour and
can't figure out how to get it to see the rest that are out there
somewhere. There is no /apt/sources.list because there is no apt. I can
google and read the how-tos and wikis, but it takes hours to find the
right information. :(

I also need to get the Ubuntu laptop and the Fedora desktop talking to
each other. Right now they can't even see each other. More googling.

to add the repo and doing it by hand isn't too hard either.

repo's packages installed by default http://www.burdell.org/?p=547

I think that they can ping each other, but I'm not sure what they're
actually pinging. If I do ifconfig on the laptop I get:

eth0      Link encap:Ethernet  HWaddr 00:0F:B0:03:03:86  
          inet addr:192.168.1.101  Bcast:192.168.1.255
Mask:255.255.255.0 inet6 addr: fe80::20f:b0ff:fe03:386/64 Scope:Link
          UP BROADCAST RUNNING MULTICAST  MTU:1492  Metric:1
          RX packets:12871111 errors:0 dropped:0 overruns:0 frame:0
          TX packets:11706175 errors:0 dropped:0 overruns:0 carrier:0
          collisions:0 txqueuelen:1000 
          RX bytes:9680739701 (9.0 GiB)  TX bytes:10485009259 (9.7 GiB)
          Interrupt:185 Base address:0xc800

On the desktop I get the same thing, except that the inet address is
192.168.1.100 and the inet6 address is completely different. The
problem is that I think 192.168.1.100 is the address of my router. So
when the laptop pings 192.168.1.100 it is just going to the router, not
the desktop. An even bigger problem is that I have no idea what I'm
talking about. :(

But I do know that both computers can go to the internet.

Your router is likely 192.168.1.1 ... from the looks of the address scheme
you have a Linksys ;-) but I could be wrong.

Those are both valid addresses, so if you they can ping each other then they
can do other things like share files via samba or NFS. So they are
networked, just a matter of what you want them to do.

Don't worry about the IPV6 addresses, they aren't used in the greater
networking world as of yet (some providers have deployed it but its rare)
but Linux does have support for it in the unlikely event you are on an IPV6
network.

Drew-

> >           TX packets:11706175 errors:0 dropped:0 overruns:0 carrier:0
> >           collisions:0 txqueuelen:1000
> >           RX bytes:9680739701 (9.0 GiB)  TX bytes:10485009259 (9.7 GiB)
> >           Interrupt:185 Base address:0xc800
> >
> > On the desktop I get the same thing, except that the inet address is
> > 192.168.1.100 and the inet6 address is completely different. The
> > problem is that I think 192.168.1.100 is the address of my router. So
> > when the laptop pings 192.168.1.100 it is just going to the router, not
> > the desktop. An even bigger problem is that I have no idea what I'm
> > talking about. :(
> >
> > But I do know that both computers can go to the internet.
>
>
> Your router is likely 192.168.1.1 ... from the looks of the address scheme
> you have a Linksys ;-) but I could be wrong.
>
> Those are both valid addresses, so if you they can ping each other then they
> can do other things like share files via samba or NFS. So they are
> networked, just a matter of what you want them to do.
>
> Don't worry about the IPV6 addresses, they aren't used in the greater
> networking world as of yet (some providers have deployed it but its rare)
> but Linux does have support for it in the unlikely event you are on an IPV6
> network.
>
> Drew-

You don't say what sort of sharing you are trying to do.  Be aware
that Fedora comes with a firewall which is enabled by default.  You
can see/edit the basic firewall settings with a GUI called
system-config-securitylevel, through the Gnome menus
System->Administration->Security Level and Firewall.

But mainly you have to be more specific about "sharing" if you want
any help here :-)

As Drew pointed out, your addressing looks fine, and if you can reach
the internet from both systems then you don't have one set to the
router's IP address.

Regarding repositories, I am not sure if the Extras repository is
rebuilt to work with testing images.  Also, one of the "features" of
Fedora 7 is the elimination of the distinction between Fedora Core and
Fedora Extras, so there may be some problems associated with that
work.  Also note that Pirut comes with Fedora, and while not as nice
as Synaptic, should show you the packages available in a GUI.  Adding
a repository manually in /etc/yum.repos.d is not too hard, and once it
is enabled there it will just work with any of the yum-based tools.

Jason

> Regarding repositories, I am not sure if the Extras repository is

The "official" repos are Core and Extras then of course there are 3rd party.
Of which Livna is the one thats most used and most useful. I've found a few
other small one's but they're for very specific things like XGL. Between
Core, Extras and Livna you'll have a hard time not getting what you want. If
they don't have it, then you can always use freshrpms.net and rpmfind for
specific packages that you might need.


I was aware that Fedora 7 merges the Core and the Extras. But I didn't
> know about /etc/yum.repos.d. Is that the equivalent
> of /apt/sources.list? If so, where do I find a list of repositories so
> I can add them?
>
> And that reminds me of another thing that would make my life easier.
> How can I fix Fedora so I don't have to type /sbin/ in front of just
> about every command? That is getting tiresome. A link? (Not an expert
> on links.) A dos-like path statement? If so, where does it go?


cd to your home dir and then edit or add the file .bash_login

in that file type the following

export PATH=$PATH:/sbin

save the file and then when you open a new terminal you should be good to
go, if don't want to close and re-open a terminal you can type

source .bash_login and you'll be good to go.

Well, I'm not a perl hacking guru, but I have frequently found a
clue stick within the perldocs. Typing "perldoc Test" gives me more than
a screenful of info, including these tidbits:
         ok(0); # ok, expected failure (see todo list, above)
         ok(1); # surprise success!
I've found "perldoc $module" to be useful when I'm trying to figure out
a module, and "perldoc -f split" to be useful when I've (sigh)
forgotten the syntax for split() (again).

From russell at personaltelco.net  Tue Mar 27 09:39:29 2007
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 27 Mar 2007 09:39:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Personal Telco monthly meeting
Message-ID: <861wja4p4u.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


Because of the cross-over interest, I thought I'd post this here.
Sorry for the noise.

Contact: Russell Senior                          FOR IMMEDIATE RELEASE
Email: info at unwirepdx-watch.org


COMMUNITY WIRELESS RESEARCHERS PRESENT RESULTS OF METROFI WIFI TESTING
       Preliminary results of an independent evaluation will be
               presented at the Personal Telco Project
                Monthly Meeting on March 28 at 6:30pm


During the Summer of 2006, the City of Portland signed an agreement
with MetroFi, Inc (Mountain View, California) to build a city-wide
wireless network that promised to provide ad-supported, free-of-charge
wifi Internet service to Portland.  As part of the 82-page agreement,
MetroFi agreed to initially deploy their network service in a
Proof-of-Concept area.  Expansion beyond the Proof-of-Concept network
was to be contingent on an independent confirmation that the network
performed up to the standards set out in the agreement.  The City
solicited bids to perform the independent evaluation and awarded a
contract to Uptown Services (Boulder, Colorado).

The results of the official Uptown Services evaluation of the MetroFi
Proof-of-Concept wireless network are expected in the next few days.

In addition to the official test, two local researchers and community
wireless activists, Caleb Phillips and Russell Senior, have performed
their own evaluation, and are ready to present some preliminary
results Wednesday evening during the monthly General Meeting of the
Personal Telco Project, a local community wireless networking
educational non-profit organization.

Among the preliminary results presented will be detailed maps showing
the boundaries of the Proof-of-Concept network as they compare with
MetroFi coverage claims, the locations of the access points,
street-level signal levels found in an extensive survey, a statistical
assessment of how well the network measures up to the expectation of a
connection in 90% of outdoor areas based on a random sample of
locations, as well as a general discussion of what they have learned
about the MetroFi network during their evaluation.

The preliminary results will be followed by a more complete report
when it is finished in the coming days.  The report will be available
for download at www.unwirepdx-watch.org.

The monthly meeting of the Personal Telco Project will be held at
Urban Grind Coffee at 2214 NE Oregon St, on Wednesday, March 28th,
starting at 6:30 pm.

                                # # #


-- 
Russell Senior
russell at unwirepdx-watch.org
http://www.unwirepdx-watch.org

On 3/27/07, Eric Wilhelm <scratchcomputing at gmail.com> wrote:
> # from Larry Brigman
> # on Tuesday 27 March 2007 09:07 am:
>
> >I am trying to do some process testing automation and was looking
> >to use Test::Simple or Test::More. I am driving this testing via
> > Net:Telnet and there are places in the code that if I time out I just
> > want to end which I do with die() but the Test:: module catches this
> > and just stops, no exit.
> >I have to kill it with a ctrl-C.
>
> Post a snippet of code that demonstrates the problem?

It seems to not allow exits after emulator_start and ok() catches the
die() mode of $telnet
on timeout.
#!/bin/sh

if [ ! -f utm_x86_bridge.env ] ; then
        echo "Unable to source the test environment to continue running!"
        exit 1
fi
source utm_x86_bridge.env

`perl -e 1 > /dev/null 2>&1` && eval 'exec perl -x $0 $*'
#!perl -l -w
#use strict;
use Test::Simple tests => 15;
  use Cwd;
  use File::Compare;
  use Net::Telnet;

sub emulator_start() {
        ok(chdir( $QEMU_PATH), 'path to emulator shell') or
        diag('cannot change path to emulator, cannot continue');
        ok(open($QEMU, $QEMU_SCRIPT), 'start emulator') or
        diag('no emulator script to start, cannot continue');
        $EMU_START = 1;
        # now wait for things to startup
        print "Sleeping, waiting for emulator/kernel/userspace startup\n";
        sleep $EMU_STARTUP_TIME;
}

ok($telnet = new Net::Telnet( Timeout=>10, Prompt=>$prompt,Errmode=>'my_die'),
        'loading telnet obj');
$telnet->input_log('test_dump.txt');
if ($ENV{UTM_TOP} eq "") {
        die "No UTM environment.  Cannot continue";
}
if ($ENV{UTM_TARGET} ne "586") {
        die "This script is only functionaly for x86 targets.";
}
emulator_start;
session_login;
do_mount;
ns_env;
go_test_location;
load_test_list;

#test loops
for (@TESTS) {
        my_chdir( $_ );
        load_sim;
        load_config("$_","$_.xml");
        run_test("$_.tcl");
        compare_output("$_.nam","$_.nam.validated");
        go_test_location;
}
#end of tests
emulator_stop;
__END__

You only need to log out and then back the first time after having created
the .bash_login ... everytime you login after that you should be fine in
terminals and any other program that relies on $PATH. You probably just need
to chmod 744 the script in order to get rid of the permissions issues.

NFS isn't too rough I'm sure you'll have it down by the end of the day

> We would reach more people.  We could tailor the talk to the subject at
> hand instead of "fill X minutes".
>

Personally, I'd say "go for it" with the understanding that things
probably won't work out as you hope.  That way if things do work out,
you are overjoyed.  And if they don't, you are prepared to learn from
the mistakes.  You may discover that even if it does not work the first
time, that it can work with some adjustment.  On the other hand, you may
discover that it will only work if you invest more resources than you
have at your disposal.

Can't hurt to try.

Regards,
- Robert

"drew wymore" <drew.wymore at gmail.com> dijo:

> > #!/bin/bash

> You only need to log out and then back the first time after having created
> the .bash_login ... everytime you login after that you should be fine in
> terminals and any other program that relies on $PATH. You probably just need
> to chmod 744 the script in order to get rid of the permissions issues.

That did it! I used Nautilus to do it -- just selected the script, then
on Properties, and under Permissions I noted that I had not set it to
be launched as a program. After checking that box, it opens a terminal
window, and commands like ifconfig work right away. Don't need to log
out and back in again.

Thanks for the chmod tip. The error message said I didn't have
permission, but what it should have said was that the script was not
executable.

Now off to study NFS. :)

Subject: [PLUG] Re: [lug] VMWare question: How to start vmware guests
	automatically
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.44.0703011957560.3247-100000@vogon.deatech.com>
References: <eac729460703010104o221675a4odc3949eee4359980@mail.gmail.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.44.0703011957560.3247-100000@vogon.deatech.com>
Message-ID: <eac729460703271543j65d80d3gc07dd699fc4fc91@mail.gmail.com>

I want to thank everyone who responded to my question about starting
vmware guests.  I also wanted to let interested parties know the
solution I decided to use.

I tried xen and altho it worked well, it didn't seem perfect for my
situation.  I will probably use it in the future, but I decided
against it this time.

I had a copy of VMWare workstation and it did everything I needed and
more; but was difficult to automate in a server situation.  I did
manage to do this by creating a user, called vm, that automatically
logged in at the console at system boot and then adding commands using
"su vm -C" and "vmrun -display 0.0 ..." to the boot.local script that
would start my virtual machines and forward the X windows to the
console.  This worked well, but was totally hokey.

In the end, I rebuilt the host system (to update everything and fix
various inefficiencies) and installed vmware server.  It is a good
product.  It is easy to use and does absolutely everything I need to
do and does it efficiently.

Thank you for all the helpful advice; and a special thank you to Dan
Carrere who spent a couple days helping me with these and other
problems.

From donkjunk at softhome.net  Wed Mar 28 08:53:01 2007

Subject: [PLUG] Re: printer won't print PDF's (resolved)

I am using ubuntu 6.10 and am printing using cups with the foomatic 
gutenprint driver. The garbage does look like a translation issue because I 
can read some of it and it looks like file formatting/data. Anyways, I 
figured out a workaround though I'm still not 100% certain what's going on. 
I found out that some (but not all) PDF's downloaded from the internet do 
this and it only happens when I print "all" pages. If I specify a print 
range it prints without any issues (even if that range happens to be every 
page in the file). This works for files I only plan on using once. For files 
I need to use multiple times I simply print the entire range to PDF which 
creates a PDF that can print all. Strangely enough printing to PDF requires 
specifying a range as printing all pages to PDF just makes a corrupt PDF.

From ali.corbin at gmail.com  Wed Mar 28 09:37:23 2007
From: ali.corbin at gmail.com (Ali Corbin)
Date: Wed, 28 Mar 2007 09:37:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DST warning
In-Reply-To: <200703120727.25453.griffint@pobox.com>
References: <200703120727.25453.griffint@pobox.com>
Message-ID: <965f90c20703280937qef3b767g15efc8ad176c7efc@mail.gmail.com>

On 3/12/07, Terry Griffin <griffint at pobox.com> wrote:
> Hi all,
>
> I just discovered that crond on my (no longer supported) FC3 system is still
> on standard time. Everything else seems to be on daylight time. I don't have
> time to track it down at the moment but I thought I should warn you.
>
> Terry

We're coming a bit late to the party.  But one of my coworkers just discovered
something similar happening on an RH80 box.  Being rather anal, er, uh,
focused, he did some investigation.  I'm inserting his report below.

===========================================
Here's a "heads up" for tech support.  There's nothing to do to
"fix" this except reboot machines.

I'll bet Congress never thought of this one:

The ctime() function reads time zone information once and stores it
in static variables.  So, if you start a program, the program calls
ctime(), then you change the zoneinfo files, say by loading a patch,
then the program calls ctime() again, the second call to
ctime() will use the same zoneinfo that it used the first time.
============================================

Again, this was on an archaic distro.  ctime() may have been fixed
to re-check tz info in the meantime.

Ali

On Fedora 7 test2 whatever fix was used ended up breaking it. I can't
get the time zone setting to stick. No matter what time zoneI set it to
it stubbornly insists that I am in London. The only way to get it to
show the correct time is to turn off internet time and set the time
manually on the clock to whatever it is here in Portland.

I started out with even bigger problems -- the clock was running double
speed on my new AMD64/2 4600 computer. I finally found a fix -- add
"noapic nolapic" to the kernel line in menu.lst. Not only did that fix
the clock, but double-clicking with the mouse now works too.

Back to trying to figure out NFS.

Why not?  (assuming permissions are set correctly, allowing
execution.)  Don't you launch executable shell scripts from a terminal
in the same way you launch binaries?  (granted, you *could* run "sh
<script>" for every script you use.. but that's why we have +x perms,
right?) Am I missing something important about shell scripts in this
context?

Debian, at least, mixes executable shell scripts in with binaries in
/sbin, and I'm assuming other places:

sfdisk:                  ELF 64-bit LSB executable, AMD x86-64,
version 1 (SYSV),
shadowconfig:            Bourne shell script text executable
shorewall:               Bourne shell script text executable
shutdown:                ELF 64-bit LSB executable, AMD x86-64,
version 1 (SYSV),
....
update-grub:             Bourne shell script text executable
update-modules:          Bourne shell script text executable
update-modules.modutils: Bourne shell script text executable
....

--Rogan

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Wed Mar 28 12:47:26 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Wed, 28 Mar 2007 12:47:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Starting a Shell Script from Konqueror's File Browser
In-Reply-To: <c834ec80703281157k75c1bcadxab4a12d9d12fa99@mail.gmail.com>
References: <460A8E58.3070108@comcast.net>
	<200703281145.33948.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<c834ec80703281157k75c1bcadxab4a12d9d12fa99@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <200703281247.26980.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Rogan Creswick
# on Wednesday 28 March 2007 11:57 am:

>> Ugh. ?I hope not. ?At least not by default please.
>
>Why not? ?(assuming permissions are set correctly, allowing
>execution.) ?Don't you launch executable shell scripts from a terminal
>in the same way you launch binaries?

Any file manager shouldn't be launching executable scripts *by default*.  
That's too much like windows.  A sane default action to be "open with a 
text editor."  For the "I just want to run it" crowd, desktop entries 
are much nicer and get to have meaningful icons.

You can set yours however you like, but given that most scripts are not 
graphical, it doesn't make a lot of sense to execute them from a 
filemanager.  I suppose it would be nice to have some way to configure 
it based on whether it is in a $PATH directory (or even symlinked to 
such a file.)

--Eric
-- 
software:  a hypothetical exercise which happens to compile.
---------------------------------------------------
    http://scratchcomputing.com
---------------------------------------------------


From griffint at pobox.com  Wed Mar 28 13:13:40 2007
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Wed, 28 Mar 2007 13:13:40 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Starting a Shell Script from Konqueror's File Browser
In-Reply-To: <200703281247.26980.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <460A8E58.3070108@comcast.net>
	<200703281145.33948.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<c834ec80703281157k75c1bcadxab4a12d9d12fa99@mail.gmail.com>
	<200703281247.26980.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <56606.10.8.0.2.1175112820.squirrel@chinook>

> # from Rogan Creswick
> # on Wednesday 28 March 2007 11:57 am:
>
>>> Ugh.  I hope not.  At least not by default please.
>>
>>Why not?  (assuming permissions are set correctly, allowing
>>execution.)  Don't you launch executable shell scripts from a terminal
>>in the same way you launch binaries?
>
> Any file manager shouldn't be launching executable scripts *by default*.
> That's too much like windows.  A sane default action to be "open with a
> text editor."  For the "I just want to run it" crowd, desktop entries
> are much nicer and get to have meaningful icons.
>
> You can set yours however you like, but given that most scripts are not
> graphical, it doesn't make a lot of sense to execute them from a
> filemanager.  I suppose it would be nice to have some way to configure
> it based on whether it is in a $PATH directory (or even symlinked to
> such a file.)
>

Normally what I do for shell scripts that I want to be able to run
from a file manager is give them the .sh file name extension. Then I
configure the file manager to run .sh files when clicked. This has a
couple of advantages. First, just by looking at the file name it is
obvious what type of file it is (and what will happen when I click it),
and second the "regular" shell scripts that don't have .sh extensions
will not get run when clicked. You can get the best of both worlds
through the magic of symbolic links.

Terry

> need to enable the execute  permissions for your user/group.)

It might be trying to work when I left-click. It may be that there is a
different problem. Here's what I've done so far. The script, named
convert-640, is as follows:

-------------------------------------------------------------------------
#!/bin/bash
for i in *jpg;
do
convert -size 640x640 -resize 640x640 $i 640/`basename $i .jpg`-640.jpg;
done
-------------------------------------------------------------------------

I use it to make smaller copies of the photos I download from my camera.

When I left-click on it "nothing" happens. When I right-click on it and
choose Open With Other, and enter bash in the text bar, "nothing"
happens. When I check "Run in terminal" and "Do not close when command
exits" I get the following error message:

convert: unable to open file `*jpg': No such file or directory.

If I open a terminal window, navigate to the directory containing the
script, and type the command, ./convert-640 it runs correctly, placing a
reduced size copy of all the .jpg files in that directory into a
sub-directory named 640 (I manually created the sub-directory).

This leads me to suspect that there is some tweaking needed somewhere.

I like the idea Terry recommended about putting the .sh extension on
shell scripts I want executed by clicking on them. Once I figure out
what else needs tweaking, I'll apply that modification, too.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

It "works" for me, here's how: I use adblock with the option to turn on 
the adblock tabs for all <object> tags, right clicking on these tags 
will allow me to "copy link location".  On the KGW video popup window 
they've embedded the video in an iframe but I can see 1 pixel of the 
adblock object tab which is enough to right click on, copy the link, 
middle click and paste it into the same window.  Since they seem to 
transmit the proper mime type firefox knows to open it up in VLC which 
plays it just fine.

On Sun, 18 Mar 2007, Larry Brigman wrote:

> Most java programs also expect at least JAVA_HOME to point to the 
> location of the base of where the java SDK.

JAVA_HOME is needed only occasionally, but the path issue is critical,
as we've seen.  You normally should set up both variables when
installing a new version of java.  It seems lame, but I think it's
better that way.  Gentoo tries to handle everything itself, and they've
made a complete mess of that, with java-config-1 and java-config-2,
several config's in /etc, and a lot of compatibility issues.  After my
last upgrade, I now have to change some of those config's manually, even
though they say "generated by java-config" in them.

--Jason

This morning I did some more installing/uninstalling of different distros (all 64-bit). I can now say with some confidence that the mouse problem is either in Gnome or in the nv driver. I had Simply Mepis loaded, which uses KDE and the video was Vesa -- not a single mouse problem for over an hour of poking around. I removed it because its kernel was too old to handle the gigabit and sound, and I couldn't find any way to upgrade the kernel without a net connection. 

I followed that with OpenSUSE 10.2, but its kernel was also too old for the gigabit and sound (2.6.18-2). I replaced it with Mandriva-free-2007. MF2007 properly identified the network card as a Realtek RTL-8169SC Gigabit Ethernet (rev 10). But it couldn't get it working. So then I went back to Fedora 7. The installation didn't specifically say it found the Realtek, but the installation went smoothly. However, when I rebooted it hung on Starting Networking, the same as it did before. Mouse and keyboard dead. It didn't set up a failsafe mode in Grub, but I went in and edited out the rghb as you suggested. This got me to:

Code: 41 8b 5d 00 85 db 0f 88 dc 03 00 00 f7 c3 00 20 00 74 60
RIP [<ffffffff8816f3e5>]  :r8169:rt8169_rx_interrup+0x5d/0x50e
RSP <ffff810037c97e10>
CR2: 0000000000000000
Kernel panic - not syncing: Aiee, killing interrupt handler!

Now, there is one thing that hits me between the eyes in the above -- the "8169." The Realtek is an RTL-8169SC. So it looks like what appeared on the graphical screen as a hang is a kernel panic caused by using the wrong IRQ for the Realtek.

So for my next exercise I decided to edit Grub some more. I tried irqpoll, but it still hung -- in a different place, but hung nevertheless. In the process I googled around and discovered that Realtek has a driver to make the 8169SC work with amd64 kernels older than 2.6.19-2. I downloaded it and it has a Readme with instructions. So I think what I am going to do next is go back to FC6 and then, instead of the fix that I found on the Fedora forums, I'll try installing the new driver.

From robert.citek at gmail.com  Thu Mar 29 05:22:44 2007
From: robert.citek at gmail.com (Robert Citek)
Date: Thu, 29 Mar 2007 07:22:44 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] Knoppix hard drive clone
In-Reply-To: <460B6153.6080509@spiretech.com>
References: <460B6153.6080509@spiretech.com>
Message-ID: <460BAF94.1040608@gmail.com>

Paul Johnston wrote:
> I want to back-up my Win2K/Linux hard drive by cloning it.  I will use
> Knoppix, and I have a second hard drive that is EXACTLY the same as the
> original.
> 
> My configuration is the hard drive is "Master" on the primary IDE
> channel, and my CD/RW is "Master" on the secondary IDE channel.
> 
> I appeal to all of you IDE Yodas out there.  How should I configure my
> back-up hard drive?  Should it be "primary SLAVE", "secondary SLAVE," or
> OTHER?

If understand correctly, you want to image your primary master drive
(/dev/hda) onto another IDE drive, either /dev/hdb or /dev/hdd.  If so
then either will work, although you'll probably get better performance
if you put the second drive as secondary slave, /dev/hdd.

Good luck and be sure to let us know how it goes.

Regards,
- Robert

Eric Wilhelm wrote:

> Pretty sure the pwd is ~ there.  That's just how kde does it.

This is getting me closer. It led me to try putting the directory 640
and a test image in my home directory. Then, while in another directory,
I single clicked on convert-640 (in that other directory) while watching
~/640 in another Konqueror window. Sure enough, the reduced size image
appeared in ~/640. So, the script works from Konqueror, but not the same
way it would work if I was looking at it in a terminal window.

> Try cd $(dirname $0) or so if you want,...

My scripting is very rusty. I'm not sure what that is supposed to
accomplish. I put it verbatim into the script, but it didn't change
anything about the way the script ran -- it still put a reduced sized
image into ~/640. The code as it now stands:

----------------------------------------------------------------------
#!/bin/bash
cd $(dirname $0)
for i in *jpg;
do
convert -size 640x640 -resize 640x640 $i 640/`basename $i .jpg`-640.jpg;
done
----------------------------------------------------------------------

>... but maybe better to just add an action 
> to konqueror that lets you right-click on a directory and say "open 
> with shrink_jpg" at which point the script would get an argument which 
> is the absolute path to a directory.

I guess my question now is, how do I get Konqueror to run a script in
the directory where the script lives?

I suppose the better solution would be something like you recommend, and
I would keep the copy of the script in one place, instead of moving it
to the new collection of photos each time I download them. It also would
be a good time to add the code to test for the presence of the directory
into which I plan to put the reduced sized images and create it if it
doesn't exist. But, I still need to know how to get the script, when
invoked through Konqueror, to know where it is.
