# from Richard C. Steffens
# on Thursday 29 March 2007 01:46 pm:

>> Try cd $(dirname $0) or so if you want,...

>My scripting is very rusty. I'm not sure what that is supposed to
>accomplish.

$0 is the name of the script.  $() is backticks.  The rest easily found 
in in manpages.

(btw:  $1 is the first argument, you'll want that later.)

>I put it verbatim into the script, but it didn't change 
>anything about the way the script ran -- it still put a reduced sized
>image into ~/640. The code as it now stands:

Where did you put the script?  ~/ ?

I've just noticed that konqueror behaves differently WRT to cwd 
depending on the profile.  E.g. if you click on the browser button, 
you'll get the pwd:=~ behavior and the filemanager icon will give you a 
dirname $0 behavior.  And, executable scripts don't seem to be 
configurable via the standard mechanism.  Bah.  I guess I can get it to 
run perl scripts this way if I make a symlink and use #!/bin/shperl as 
the shebang... I guess the same goes for shlisp and shobol too :-D

Ok, that's enough obscure amusement for one day.

I really do think the "open directory with this program" mechanism would 
be best.  Add an entry for your script using the menu editor 
(right-click on the "K"-menu) then right-click on the directory and go 
to properties, then click the "type" wrench and add an association.

Note, that trick works great for gqview too.

--Eric

From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri Mar 30 22:27:32 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Fri, 30 Mar 2007 22:27:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Rip music on two CDs
Message-ID: <20070330222732.f7f342ff.johnxj@comcast.net>

I have a long classical piece I want to rip and encode with cdparanoia
and lame. However, it was too long to fit on one CD, so they put it on
two CDs, each having "track 1." Does anyone know of any way to rip them
to wav files and then marry the wav files when encoding? Or rip both
tracks to one wav file?


From ryan.mannon at gmail.com  Sat Mar 31 01:28:35 2007
From: ryan.mannon at gmail.com (Ryan)
Date: Sat, 31 Mar 2007 01:28:35 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Rip music on two CDs
In-Reply-To: <20070330222732.f7f342ff.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20070330222732.f7f342ff.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <a4ba175e0703310128o148d5ac6s95aebd6174820d06@mail.gmail.com>

On Kubuntu (or any other KDE based distro), I would use KaudioCreator to rip
the cd's to wav files and then load the files into Audacity.  You can splice
things together very nicely in Audacity and then encode it to an mp3.  Just
make sure you have something like lame installed to encode the file. Here
are a couple of links:

http://audacity.sourceforge.net/

http://www.icefox.net/programs/?program=KAudioCreator

Should be pretty straight forward if you read the faq's for both programs.
Hope that helps.

-ryan

Michael Dexter wrote:

> I am working with a local university and I would to profile what's is going on back home. Both from memory and a little research I have come up with:
<...>
> Whatever it is Google is building in the Dallas.

I believe it is a big data center, taking advantage of the commercial
power rates available so close to the source - The Dalles dam.

BTW, it's The Dalles, not to be confused with Dallas, which is closer to
Salem, and not on the Columbia river. In my tour guiding days I learned
that the name comes from French Canadian and means something like paving
stones. The stretch of the Columbia River running by The Dalles includes
 a geologic feature that is supposed to have looked like paving stones
to the French Canadian fur traders. Bonneville dam created a lake that
covered the chasm so that now, all that remains is the name.

On Sat, 31 Mar 2007 01:28:35 -0700
Ryan <ryan.mannon at gmail.com> dijo:

> On Kubuntu (or any other KDE based distro), I would use KaudioCreator to rip
> the cd's to wav files and then load the files into Audacity.  You can splice
> things together very nicely in Audacity and then encode it to an mp3.

Audacity! Why didn't I think of that? I've used it a lot for work in
phonetics, but I never used its ability to join sound files.

I'm doing the rest of my CD collection on my new AMD64/2 computer with
cdparanoia and lame, running on Fedora 7 test2 amd64. Lame takes about
4 minutes to encode an entire CD. I opened the System Monitor to watch
what resources it's using. It's very interesting that it takes 100% of
one CPU and about 20-30% of the other one, but it swaps back and forth
so that sometimes the 100% is on CPU0 and sometimes it's on CPU1.

The time-consuming part is ripping with cdparanoia, which never goes
above 5% CPU usage on either CPU, nor does it seem to use much RAM. It
does a great job, but it doesn't run any faster than it does on my
two-year old laptop.

find that my 48x plextor burner rips about 4x the speed of my 52x

having the fast one in an external USB case.  Maybe the driver or just 
a doggy onboard IDE?

Hmm. I hadn't thought of the CD drive as being the limiting factor. Now
that I think of it I notice that the light on the drive is on
constantly while cdparanoia is ripping. I put two LG DVD drives in this
computer, both are18x "Super Multi DVD Rewriter" drives with
LightScribe. They are ATAPI and the one I'm ripping from is the master
(the other one is not used at the same time). But cdparanoia doesn't
show what speed it is ripping at. It just shows a progress bar and the
track number is constantly incremented. I'm doing it from the command
line.

If the drive speed is the bottleneck, that would partially explain why
lame runs so much faster, as it's reading the file off the SATA-II hard
drive. And that would also explain why no one has bothered to make
cdparanoia multithreaded, or use more RAM or CPU speed.

>show what speed it is ripping at. It just shows a progress bar and the
>track number is constantly incremented. I'm doing it from the command

You can calculate that by timing the process (`man time`) and looking at 
the size of the wav.  A 650MB wav would take 72min at 1x.  I imagine 
this is what grip does (though it might have a more internal hook to 
cdparanoia.)  Or just do some tests in grip.

John Jason Jordan said the following on 03/30/2007 10:27 PM:
> I have a long classical piece I want to rip and encode with cdparanoia
> and lame. However, it was too long to fit on one CD, so they put it on
> two CDs, each having "track 1." Does anyone know of any way to rip them
> to wav files and then marry the wav files when encoding? Or rip both
> tracks to one wav file?

I don't know if it works with wav files, but I do it with mp3s all the 
time using cat.

$ cp first.mp3 onebig.mp3
$ cat second.mp3 >> onebig.mp3

Listen to onebig.mp3

John

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Thu Mar 29 16:46:33 2007
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Thu, 29 Mar 2007 16:46:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Why does hardware lock up???
Message-ID: <20070329164633.fwjekail28gk8so4@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Someone has been trying to run a Realtek gigabit
ethernet card and running into trouble with it
locking up.

In general, why do some hardware devices lock the Linux
kernel up?

I've heard IRQ conflicts and the like, but haven't
IRQ's been kinda hokey since the dawn of PCI?  Doesn't
the IRQ system go back to the 80's when PC's were first
coming out, back when 640k of memory was considered
ample?  Anyone need 2 gigabytes of ram to run their Vista
box ;-)  It's been a long time since 15 interrupts could
accomodate every hardware device on a PC easily.  Indeed,
especially with the way most sound cards waste resources
and with the added resource load of USB, IRQ sharing is
very common these days.

I expect with 64 bit platforms that the old: IRQ, memory
mapping, and port mapping standards are going to be
replaced.  One option I can think of is making the
expansion bus a serial line running at 8 Gbps.

I'm curious why Linux can't avoid locking up in some cases
if for example the network card is poorly supported or
the sound card is problematic?  I've had problems myself
with a RealTek 8180 pci wireless card locking up my Linux
system.  I am not very impressed with Realtek in general.

Michael C. Robinson
